?⌟jīvaḥ saṁd*ṛśyate, sadā ca dhyānāhāraḥ* (Tib. *bsam gtan gyi dga' ba'i* zas za ba).**

**1650 Unabhängig von einem Fleischverzehr-Verbot: MPM \# 578,8-19 (s. En. 1776). Im Kontext einer Argumentation gegen Fleischverzehr: LaṅkSN 255,12-14 (s. Text III: D.5.2).**

**1651 Vgl. MPM \# 175,18-21 (Text I: B.8). 1652 Eine fleischlose Ernährung als Ideal könnte sich auch im *Therāpadāna* des Pāli-Kanons und dem Kommentar dazu abzeichnen, wenn man die nach ROZENBERG (2002: 15f; s. Anhang B, § b.3) dort zu beobachtende überwiegend lacto-vegetarische Bewirtung "heiliger" Personen als die Regel und die drei von mir (ibid.) angeführten Gegenbeispiele als durch die jeweilige Spenderperson bedingte Ausnahmen versteht. Beachtung verdient in diesem Zusammenhang vielleicht auch, daß im Kommentar zum *Buddhavaṁsa* (Bv-a [PTS] 298) zwar abschließend festgestellt wird, daß bei allen Buddhas die Mahlzeit am Tage der Erlangung des "vollkommenen Erwachens" (*saṁbodhi*) in Milchreisbrei (*pāyāsa*) bestehe und die letzte Mahlzeit vor dem Eingehen ins Nirvāna in Fleischbrühe (*maṁsarasa*), in den Darstellungen des Lebens der einzelnen Buddhas aber nur die erstgenannte Mahlzeit erwähnt wird (Bv-a [PTS] 132 [Koṇḍañña], 142 [Maṅgala], 161 [Revata], etc.), die letztere hingegen nie. - Zur Interpretation der Gabe von Hirse, Blättern und Reis an einen Pratyekabuddha in Nett 141,12* s. En. 240.**

**1653"Lehrrede über hier und dort [vom Buddha angesprochene Punkte]"(?). GREENE 2016: 15: "a text whose title which is none too clear but which might be provisionally interpreted as *Scriptural Miscellany*". Hypothetischer Alternativvorschlag v. Michael Radich (briefl.) im Anschluß an T 17.730: 526b15-22:**

**"Lehrrede über die verschiedenen Orte [der Wiedergeburt]"(?).**

**1654 Die ideengeschichtliche Beurteilung des aus vielen nicht oder nur locker miteinander zusammenhängenden Abschnitten bestehenden Sūtras bedarf genauerer Untersuchung. In Fa-ching's *Chung-ching mu-lu* 衆經目錄, kompiliert im Jahre 594 (NATTIER 2008: 13), wird der Text als ein Werk des Kleinen Fahrzeugs (小乘) eingeordnet (T 55.2146: 127c21 + 137b16). Manche Abschnitte geben in der Tat Erklärungen zu bestimmten Aussagen der kanonischen Überlieferung und traditionellen Vorstellungen, z.B. warum der Körper des Buddha nach dessen Eingehen ins Nirvāṇa verbrannt wurde (T 17.730: 524b14f), oder warum der Buddha seinen Schüler Śāriputra Sūtras rezitieren läßt (524b29–c5). An einer Stelle (527a21-23) wird hingegen gesagt, daß Mönche, vom Buddha über die Bodhisattva-Praxis belehrt, die anutpattikadharmak*ṣānti* erlangten. An einer anderen Stelle (524c11-14) wiederum heißt es, daß von einer riesigen Menge von Personen, die ursprünglich zusammen den Bodhisattva-Weg zu praktizieren versuchten, nur zweien Erfolg beschieden war, nämlich Śākyamuni und Maitreya, während alle anderen lediglich Arhats oder Pratyekabuddhas wurden; vgl. auch 525a7-17.**

## Summary of the text

**§ 1655:**

* The Ch'u-ch'*u ching* is not listed among An Shih-kao's authentic translations by Nattier (2008) and Vetter (2012).
* It is also absent from Tao-an's (道安) catalog of 374, which lists An Shih-kao's works.

**§ 1656:**

* Hayashiya Tomojirō also notes the absence of the Ch'u-ch'*u ching* in early catalogs.

**§ 1657:**

* A passage from the Ch'u-ch'*u ching* states that Pratyekabuddhas understand the arising dependent origination (*pratītyasamutpāda*).
* They attain enlightenment through understanding causes and conditions (*pratyaya*).

**§ 1658:**

* The phrase "from head to foot" (從頭至足) in the Ch'u-ch'*u ching* is similar to a passage in MN I 57,13-34.

**§ 1659:**

* Pratyekabuddhas are characterized by their insight into dependent origination.
* They achieve enlightenment through understanding causes and conditions (*pratyaya*).

**§ 1660:**

* The concept of aversion to food arises from the contemplation of its origins as impure.
* Examples include meat originating from semen, blood, and the "path of urine," and dairy products being transformed from blood.

**§ 1661:**

* Greene (2016) suggests that the passage about food aversion does not necessarily reflect the daily diet of Buddhist monks and nuns.
* It implies an ideal of vegetarianism for advanced practitioners.

**§ 1662:**

* The Ch'u-ch'*u ching* describes a Pratyekabuddha who sees all worldly things as empty and impermanent.

**§ 1663:**

* A passage from the Ch'u-ch'*u ching* states that the Buddha believes everything in the world is empty and impermanent.
* He understands that existence arises and ceases, ultimately returning to emptiness.

**§ 1664:**

* Greene (2016) also interprets this passage as emphasizing emptiness and impermanence.

**§ 1665:**

* The ideal of non-attachment to material possessions is contrasted with the characterization of Rudra in the *Śatarudrīya* of the *Mahābhārata*.
* Rudra is described as a "flesh-, blood- and bone marrow-eater" (māṁsaśoṇita*majjāda*), representing destructive, violent power.

**§ 1666:**

* The phrase (不覺)虛中而過 refers to someone who is unaware of missing a meal.
* It appears in various texts, including T 55.2145 and T 50.2059.

**§ 1667:**

* The idea that the Buddha or other ascetics with supernatural abilities could obtain alms from Uttarakuru is found in various sources.
* This concept is discussed in CPD II 373a and CPS 281.

**§ 1668:**

* The term 濟 is interpreted as "finished with" or "satisfied" in this context.
* It implies a sense of contentment, sufficiency, or being satiated.

**§ 1669:**

* Two accounts of the Buddha's refusal to eat are presented: one stating he passed through emptiness, and another suggesting he obtained food from Uttarakuru.

**§ 1670:**

* T 17.730: 524b21-27 provides further context for the Buddha's refusal to eat.

**§ 1671:**


* T 17.730: 524b24f describes animals being kept alive solely for sustenance.

**§ 1672:**

* Chih Ch'ien (支謙) is possibly the translator of the *Ch'u-ch'*u ching*, according to HôbFA 277(r).
* Nattier (2008) also mentions him as a co-translator.

**§ 1673:**

* The *Fa ching lu* (法經錄) lists Chih Ch'ien as a co-translator of the *Ch'u-ch'*u ching*.
* However, Seng-yu's *Ch'u san tsang chi chi* (出三藏記集) does not mention him.

**§ 1674:**

* Greene (2016) notes that the *Ch'u-ch'*u ching* is not mentioned in Nattier (2008).

**§ 1675:**


* The argument for vegetarianism based on kinship might be influenced by vegetarian Sūtras like AṅgS or LaṅkS.

**§ 1676:**

* Several Buddhist texts discuss the idea of seeing all beings as interconnected and potentially having been one's relatives in past lives.
* This concept is used to discourage meat consumption.

**§ 1677:**


* The analogy of eating meat being like consuming one's own child highlights the interconnectedness of all beings.

**§ 1678:**

* T 17.793: 737c17-19 explains that when people crave meat, they should reflect on the fact that it could be the flesh of their former relatives.
* This contemplation can lead to a rejection of meat consumption.

**§ 1679:**


* T 17.793: 737c28f compares eating meat to consuming one's own child, emphasizing the interconnectedness of all beings.

**§ 1680:**

* See also T 1.20: 261b4 for a related passage.

**§ 1681:**


* Six conditions are listed that prohibit eating meat, including not killing oneself, others, or supporting killing.
* It also includes witnessing, hearing about, or suspecting killing done for one's sake.

**§ 1682:**

* The presence of these six conditions in the *Fo-i ching* does not necessarily imply a later date of composition compared to full *Vinaya* versions.

**§ 1683:**

* See § 19.3 and En. 163 for more information on the *Fo-i ching*.

**§ 1684:**


* T 17.793: 738a3 states that one who does not eat meat can avoid fear and attain blessings.

**§ 1685:**

* T 17.793: 738a3f suggests that those who abstain from meat are free from fear and attain blessings.

**§ 1686:**

* See T. SUZUKI 2001; 2003: 4b–5a (\#\# 4 u. 5) for further discussion.

**§ 1687:**


* T. SUZUKI 2001; 2003: 4b (\# 4) provides analysis of the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra*.

**§ 1688:**

* The structure of the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra* is discussed by HABATA (2013), SHIMODA (1997), and RADICH (2015).
* The text's history and relationship to the *Fo-i ching* are complex.

**§ 1689:**


* Chapters 8–18 of the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra* deal with meat consumption, according to SHIMODA.

**§ 1690:**

* See RADICH (2011(2012): 266f and 2015: 98) for more information on the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra*.

**§ 1691:**


* See § 192 + En. 1754 for further discussion.

**§ 1692:**

* MMeght P dzu 133b5–138a7 (S ta [Bd. 60] 18b2–24a4; D wa 124a3–128a4); MMeghc 1081a5–1082a10 present the relevant passage from the *Mahāparinirvāṇa Sūtra*.

**§ 1693:**


* MMeght P dzu 136b1f (S ta 21b4f; D wa 126b2f) describes abstaining from meat as a way to cultivate compassion for all beings.

**§ 1694:**

* MMeghc 1081a5 reiterates the importance of avoiding meat consumption.

**§ 1695:**


* MMeghc 1081c1-3 emphasizes abstaining from greed, practicing contentment, and avoiding meat to cultivate compassion for all beings.

**§ 1696:**

* MMeght P dzu 154a1-3, 158b2f u. 158b6–166b3 (S ta 45a4-6, 50b4-6 u. 51a2–60b4; D wa 142b2f, 146b2f u. 146b5–153b4), MMeghc 1086a29–1088a9 u. b3f discuss the benefits of vegetarianism.

**§ 1697:**


* MMeght P dzu 156b3 u. 169a2f (S ta 48a7 u. 63b6f; D wa 144b7 u. 155b7) highlight the connection between compassion and vegetarianism in the context of meditative practices.

**§ 1698:**


* See SUZUKI 2003, especially 4b–5a (\# 4), for further analysis.

**§ 1699:**

* MMeght P dzu 136b6 (S ta 22a3; D wa 126b6) describes the cultivation of compassion through abstaining from meat.

**§ 1700:**


* MMeght P dzu 136b7 (S ta 22a4; D wa 126b7); MMeghc 1081c5 emphasize the importance of avoiding meat consumption for spiritual progress.

**§ 1701:**

* MMeght P dzu 203b4-7 (S ta 110b5–111a1; D wa 186b2-4) lists various activities that are considered unwholesome, including owning slaves and engaging in exploitative practices.

# 1703

* This section discusses the "16 unwholesome observances" (~asaṁvara) which appear in the Tibetan version as the "16 kinds of great activities" (*las chen po rnam pa bcu drug).
* These are also mentioned in MMeghc 1080c25.

# 1704

* This section provides a quote from MMeght P dzu 1099b26-29 (see Suzuki 2003: 3a [\# 3.1(a)]) about the sixteen unwholesome observances.
* It describes how a layman named Ruṭha achieved these observances through his wealth and possessions, including livestock, grain, houses, and luxurious items.

# 1705

* This section discusses parallels for abstaining from raw grain in other Buddhist texts.
* It mentions SaṅghBhV (II 233,17: *āmadhānyapratigrahāt* ...), the Lohitya*sūtra* of the Dīrghāgama (Mūla-)Sarvāstivādins, SHT 8: 64, Kat.-Nr. 1873 V2 ((ā)madhānyāt prati*vira{..}to*), SaṅgP 407a15f, MĀc (499c14f; 552c24f; 657b16f; 733c1-3), and T 1.20 (261b8f).

# 1706

* This section discusses the absence of a parallel for abstaining from raw meat in other Buddhist texts.
* It mentions that T 1.20 (261b4) states that a true ascetic should not drink alcohol or crave meat, but notes that some versions omit the reference to meat.

# 1707

* This section discusses the translation of *dāsī/*dāsa* as "slaves" or "servants".
* It references SCHOPEN (1994 = 2004: 193–218) and BOUCHER (2008: 234 Anm. 237).

# 1708

* This section notes the absence of abstaining from errands in parallel versions, but mentions its presence in Dīrghāgama texts corresponding to DN I 66,37–67,6 (or I 8,19-27).

# 1709

* This section provides a quote from DN I 64,20-28 (DN I 5,9-20; MN I 180,8-16) listing various observances.
* It mentions parallels in SaṅghBhV (Mūlasarvāstivāda), DĀ of the Mūla-Sarvāstivādins, SaṅgP, DĀc (Dharmaguptaka), T 1.20, 1.21, 1.22, and MĀc (likely Sarvāstivāda).

# 1710

* This section quotes MMeght P dzu 204a1f (S ta [Bd. 60] 111a4f; D wa 186b6f) about a bodhisattva praising the teachings (*śikṣāpada*) and various practices like living in the wilderness and upholding monastic discipline (*vinaya*).

# 1711

* This section quotes MMeghc 1099c5-9 (see Suzuki 2003: 3a–b [\# 3_1(b)]) praising those who practice austerity and hold the precepts.
* It mentions that they are said to have achieved full Pratimoksha and are praised for their contentment with simple robes made from discarded materials.

# 1712

* This section contrasts lax monks described in MPM \# 151,20-22 with a strict preacher in MPM \# 152,14-16.

# 1713

* This section notes that MMeghc (1080c26) simply states that "all evil beings took the five *śīla*s".

# 1714

* This section quotes MMeght P dzu 133a1 (S ta [Bd. 60]: 17b2f; D wa 123a6f) about dangerous and poisonous animals entering an assembly, laying down their nature, and adopting a friendly attitude.

# 1715

* This section mentions that some lines earlier, various groups of dangerous and poisonous animals are also mentioned who enter the assembly and shed their venomous nature, taking on a compassionate disposition (MMeghc: 1080c25: *maitrī*-citta).

# 1716

* This section refers to § 181.1 + En. 1703.

# 1717

* This section quotes MMeght P dzu 132b7f (S ta 17b1; D wa 123a6) about "16 kinds of terrible activities" (*las mi bzad pa rnam pa bcu drug).




## # 1739

* This section lists six arguments against meat consumption found in the *Mahāparinirvāṇa-sūtra* (*MPM*) and the *Laṅkāvatāra-sūtra* (*LaṅkSGu*).
* The arguments are based on:
    * The stench of a meat eater repelling all beings.
    * Meat consumption hindering the development of loving-kindness (*maitrī*).
    * Even consuming the flesh of one's own son being considered unacceptable, making any other meat consumption equally so.
    * A progression from partial to complete prohibitions on meat eating.
    * The Buddhas and Bodhisattvas abstaining from all food, let alone meat.
    * Future claims that the Buddha allowed meat consumption being refuted.

## # 1740

* This section explains why the *MPM* only explicitly forbids meat consumption for Śrāvakas, while Bodhisattvas are assumed to already follow this rule.
* It cites two Chinese texts (*T 37.1764* and *T 37.1763*) that support this interpretation.

## # 1741

* This section discusses the *Tarkajvālā*, a text that doesn't mention the *MPM* but likely knew of it.
* It suggests that the author of the *Tarkajvālā* may have been familiar with an older version of the *Laṅkāvatāra-sūtra*.

## # 1742

* This section discusses the chronological placement of the *Mahāparinirvāṇa-sūtra* between other sutras.
* It argues that placing it after the *Laṅkāvatāra-sūtra* would be metrically possible.

## # 1743

* This section cites a similar argument made by Suzuki (2003) regarding the chronological placement of the *Mahāparinirvāṇa-sūtra*.

## # 1744

* This section references passages in the *MPM* that indicate a shift from allowing certain types of meat consumption to a complete prohibition.
* It mentions Shimoda's (1990a) work on this topic.

## # 1745

* This section refers to Habata's (2007) extensive analysis of the *Mahāparinirvāṇa-sūtra*'s title.

## # 1746

* This section cites Shimoda's (1990a and 1997) work on the relationship between the *Mahāparinirvāṇa-sūtra* and other Buddhist texts.

## # 1747

* This section discusses the possibility that the *MPM* assumes a pre-existing practice of vegetarianism among Buddhists, citing a passage from the *Bodhisattva Bhumi* (*BoBhD*) as evidence.
* It also mentions the potential influence of Brahmanical dietary practices on some Buddhist groups.

## # 1748

* This section reiterates the traditional view that the *MPM*, being the Buddha's final teaching, must have come after sutras like the *Aṅgulimālīya-sūtra* and the *Laṅkāvatāra-sūtra*.

## # 1749

* This section explains the chapter divisions of the *MPM* in different translations.
* It notes variations between the "northern" and "southern" versions of Dharmakṣema's Chinese translation.

## # 1750

* This section provides details about the two main versions of Dharmakṣema's Chinese translation of the *MPM*.
* It mentions the existence of a fragmentary alttürkic translation.

## # 1751

* This section refers to Habata's (2007) work on the different versions of the *MPM*.

## # 1752

* This section cites Yuyamas (1981) and Habata's (2007) work on the textual history of the *MPM*.
* It indicates that the author is using Habata's (2013) critical edition of the text.

## # 1753

* This section acknowledges Habata's (2007 and 2019) contributions to reconstructing fragments of the *MPM*.




## Summary

**# 1768 MPM \# 174,17-21 (Text I: B.4.2.b). 1769 MPM \# 175,2-7 (Text I: B.6)**

* These passages discuss the inclusion of meat from animals that died naturally in the prohibition against eating meat.
* They reference Mhbh 13.116.37 for further context.

**# 1770 MPM \# 175,18-21 (Text I: B.8)**

* This section discusses the consumption of meat as an *upāya* (skillful means) by bodhisattvas.
* It refers to T 62.2247: 177c9-15 for additional information.

**# 1771 MPM \# 567,7–568,4 (Dha 427a15-23)**

* This passage recounts a story of the Buddha being offered meat by a hunter and accepting it due to his compassion for all beings.
* The Buddha urges the hunter to consider the fear and love for life that animals experience, similar to his own feelings towards his son Rāhula.

**# 1772 Zu dieser Implikation von *nitya* im MPM vgl. HABATA 2019: I.3.**

* This section suggests consulting HABATA 2019: I.3 for further information on the implication of *nitya* in the MPM.

**# 1773 MPM \# 144 (Fa 866a16-18; Dha 382c27-29): ... *de bzhin gshegs pa rtag pa'i* sku dang | mi shigs pa'i sku dang | rdo rje'i sku dang | *sha'i sku ma yin pa dang* | chos kyi skur ltos shig |; \# 146,4-7 (Fa 866a23f): de bzhin gshegs pa ni ... sha'i lus ma yin te |; \# 148,4f (Fa 866b27f; Dha 383b17): *de bzhin gshegs pa ni chos kyi* sku ste | rtag pa dang | brtan pa dang | ther zug pa dang | *zhi ba'o ...* |; \# 196,11f (Fa 871a2; Dha 388c3f; the Buddha speaks): *nga ni chos kyi sku yin gyis* (JPDN gyi, F no) | *rus pa dang | rgyus pa dang | sha dang | khrag gi lus ma yin la* | ...; SHIMODA 1993: 245–247; 1997: 239ff u. 20f; RADICH 2011(2012), bes. 259– 262.**

* This section discusses passages from the MPM that describe the Buddha's body as indestructible like a vajra (*vajrabhedya*).
* It explores different interpretations of this term and its significance in Buddhist thought.

**# 1774 MPM \#160,10: de bzhin gshegs pa rdo rje ltar mi shigs pa'i sku dang ldan pa... = vajrābhedya*kāyas tathā(gataḥ*) (s. HABATA 2019: II.7.7, SF 11.11; MATSUDA 1988: 30 [Verso 7]; vgl. Fa 867c9f: 金剛不壞法身; Dha 384c22f: 如 來身者 即是金剛不可壞身; Dhat P ju 55a1f: de bzhin gshegs pa'i sku ni rdo rje'i sku | mi 'jig pa'i sku ste); \# 148,6: *rdo rje lta bur mi shigs pa'i sku*. Vgl auch \# 160,14f: rdo rje ltar mi shigs pa'i sku'i phung po zhes bya ba'i le'u ste gnyis pa'o = vajrābhedyakāyo nāma dvitīya skandhaḥ *sam[āp]t[aḥ*] || (HABATA 2007: 68 [SF 12.2]; BONGARD-LEVIN 1986: 24 [Recto 1]).**

* This passage continues the discussion of *vajrabhedya* and its meaning in relation to the Buddha's body.
* It cites various sources and examines different interpretations of this term.

**# 1775 MPM \# 374,4-7 (vgl. Fa 883b6-8; Dha 407a27–b2): de la de bzhin gshegs pa rtag ste sha'i sku ma yin pa la | sha'i sku yin te | sha dang khrag dang rgyus pas sbrel ba (/pa) yin no || mi gtsang ba'o || *...* snyam pa ni gtsang ba la mi gtsang bar phyin ci log pa yin no ||; MPM \# 333,14-16 (Fa 879c21f; Dha 402a16f): de bzhin gshegs pa ni sha'i sku yin te | ... zhes bya ba 'di ni nyan thos kyi theg pa yin te.**

* This section discusses passages from the MPM that describe the Buddha's body as not being made of flesh, blood, or bone.
* It analyzes these descriptions and their implications for Buddhist understanding of the Buddha's nature.

**# 1776 MPM \# 578,8–19 (Fa 899a18-24; Dha 427c14-16): dgra bcom dang ni rang sangs rgyas || byang chub sems dpa' de bzhin gshegs || de dag rtag tu zas med de | ... zhal zas kyang mi gsol la | sbyin pa 'bum phrag mang po yang bzhes par mdzad do || ... sems can rnams sems can dmyal ba dang | dud 'gro dang | yi dags kyi rgya mtsho las nges par bsgral ba'i phyir sbyin pa'i pha rol tu phyin pa bsngags par yang mdzad la | gsol bar yang mi mdzad do ||**

* This passage discusses the Buddha's rejection of meat consumption and his emphasis on compassion for all beings.
* It cites passages from the MPM that illustrate these teachings.

* This section refers to a specific translation or commentary (Text I, Übers. En. 2746) for further information on the topic discussed in the previous passage.

**# 1779 So ausdrücklich MPM Fa 869b8 (Text I: B.10.1.c). Vgl. auch T 70.2306: 741a13f: "... a land where everyone eats meat, where there is no rice food, but only meat" (... 純食肉國土, 都無米糧, 但有肉者).**

## # 1807

* This section refers to milk as a bodily secretion and compares it to honey, which is also considered a bodily product of bees.
* It mentions Jain views on the consumption of honey due to the harm inflicted upon bees during its production.

## # 1809

* Discusses the traditional view that milk comes from the blood of cows transformed by their good karma.
* Presents different versions of this idea from Tibetan and Chinese sources, highlighting variations in wording and interpretation.

## # 1810

* Explores the concept of milk production in Buddhist texts, specifically focusing on the transformation of cow's blood into milk.
* Analyzes textual discrepancies between Tibetan and Chinese versions, suggesting possible errors or differences in understanding.

## # 1811

* Addresses the argument that consuming plant-based foods is akin to consuming animal flesh because plants are sentient beings.
* Presents a passage from a Tibetan text refuting this idea, arguing that it leads to absurd conclusions about the consumption of various foodstuffs.

## # 1812

* Discusses the meaning of the term "anujñā" in Buddhist texts, which can be translated as "permit," "approve," or "ordain."
* Explores nuances in its usage and how it relates to the Buddha's teachings on vegetarianism.

## # 1813

* Analyzes a passage from the MPM (Madhyamaka-Prajñāpāramitā) concerning the Buddha's stance on the consumption of milk, honey, silk, etc.
* Identifies textual inconsistencies and proposes possible solutions for reconciling them.
* Discusses alternative interpretations of the text based on different readings and grammatical constructions.

## # 1814

* Presents a Tibetan passage from the MPM outlining the Buddha's teachings on avoiding certain foods due to their association with harm to sentient beings.
* Includes parallel passages from Chinese versions (Fa-hsien and Dharmakṣema) for comparison.

## # 1815

* Considers the influence of Jainism on Buddhist views on vegetarianism, particularly regarding the consumption of honey.
* Expresses skepticism about the extent to which Jain practices directly influenced early Buddhism.

##  Summary of Text

**# 1851**

* This section discusses the prohibition of meat consumption for all followers (śrāvaka) of the Buddha.
* It addresses seemingly contradictory Vinaya rules that apply only to ordained monks.
* The text argues that meat consumption is incompatible with *mahāmaitrī* (great loving-kindness) due to the aura of hostility it generates in the eater.

**# 1852**

* See En. 1750.

**# 1853**

* This section discusses the distinction between *prakṛti*- and *pratikṣepaṇa*- (*prajñapti*)-*sāvadya*.
* It compares this distinction to the Theravāda tradition's differentiation of *loka*- and *paṇṇatti-vajja*.

**# 1854**

* This excerpt from MPM Dha 432c16f states that abstaining from meat and alcohol is conducive to a pleasant body odor and respect from both humans and deities.

**# 1855**

* MPM Fa 869b3f lists animals whose flesh is prohibited, including lions, tigers, panthers, mongooses, and cats.

**# 1856**

* This section discusses the term *māṭāli* (or *mārjāra*) which often refers to a cat or civet cat.
* It provides examples from various texts where this term is used.

**# 1857**

* MPM Dha 432c20-22 lists animals whose ownership and consumption are prohibited, including elephants, horses, cattle, sheep, camels, donkeys, chickens, dogs, monkeys, peacocks, parrots, and certain other creatures.

**# 1858**

* MPM Dha 517b23f describes individuals who frequent slaughterhouses, butcher shops, and the homes of Caṇḍālas (an outcaste group traditionally associated with occupations considered impure).
* This section connects meat consumption to the Caṇḍāla caste.

**# 1859**


* This section discusses the association between Caṇḍālas and meat consumption and procurement.
* It cites a passage from KHMCh that portrays Caṇḍālas as those who slaughter animals for meat eaters.

**# 1860**

* This section lists five places that are forbidden to monks according to ŚrBhSh, ŚrBhTai, and YBh: royal palaces, crowds, brothels, houses of gamblers, and the homes of Caṇḍālas.

**# 1861**

* MPM Dha 473c5f lists five types of dwellings that monks should avoid: slaughterhouses, brothels, taverns, royal palaces, and Caṇḍāla homes.

**# 1862**

* This section discusses the term *ghoṣa*, which can refer to a house of singers or musicians.
* It compares different interpretations of this term in various texts.

**# 1863**

* VinMā 487a23-25 lists ten types of meat that are prohibited: human, snake, elephant, horse, donkey, dog, lion, pig, fox/jackal, and monkey.

**# 1864**

* This section presents a passage from MPM Dha 473b29–c3 that discusses the debate surrounding the prohibition of specific types of meat for monks.
* Some argue that only ten types are forbidden, while others claim that all meat is prohibited.

**# 1865**

* MPM Dha 462a11 lists extreme ascetic practices, including fasting to death, throwing oneself into a ravine or fire, and leaping from a steep cliff.

**# 1866**

* This section describes various radical ascetic practices mentioned in the text, such as covering oneself with ashes, constantly lying on bare earth, standing on one leg, roasting one's body between five fires, and distinguishing oneself through long hair braids.

**# 1867**


* MPM Dha 462a16 states that abstaining from salt, meat, and five types of cow flavor is recommended.
* Dhat P ju 277a8 (D nya 262a4) provides a similar list of prohibited items.

**# 1868**

* DURT 1994 suggests that the text may be a product of Indianized Central Asia.

**# 1869**

* SEYFORT RUEGG 1980 and 1973 discuss the origin and meaning of the term *tathāgata*garbha*.

**# 1870**


* This section discusses the origin and meaning of the term *tathāgata*garbha*, citing various scholarly works.

**# 1871**

* AṅgSt P 164b2f (D 157a4f; S 198a2f; F 128a2-4; AṅgSc 526a27-29) states that despite their efforts, all Buddhas have been unable to find a newly arisen *tathāgata*garbha*.
* Instead, they found a Buddha-nature (*buddhadhātu*) as unoriginated, adorned with thirty-two characteristics and eighty-four secondary marks, present in all beings.

**# 1872**

* SHIMODA 1997 discusses the interpretation of *tathāgata*garbha*.

**# 1873**


* This section clarifies that the term *tathāgata*garbha* is found throughout older textual layers and even in Chapter 5 (according to Fa), which is considered differently valued.

**# 1874**

* KANŌ 2000 discusses the interpretation of the title "Aṅgulimāla Sūtra" as referring to or dedicated to Aṅgulimāla.

**# 1875**

* RADICH 2011(2012) suggests a specific dating for the text.

**# 1876**

* See Text II (Ed.), Preliminary Remarks § 1 and footnote 4.

**# 1877**


* This section notes that the text in question is likely later than the portion of MPM shared by all three versions.

**# 1878**

* RADICH 2015 discusses the challenges of establishing dependencies between texts, citing T. SUZUKI's work.
* She expresses skepticism about assumptions often made regarding such dependencies.

**# 1879**

* This section explores the potential relationship between the "meat-eating sections" of MPM and AṅgS, acknowledging shared themes but cautioning against assuming direct dependence.


**# 1880**

* AṅgSt P 200a5f (S 248a3f; AṅgSc 539a27) mentions fish and meat as strengthening, first-class foods alongside milk, sweet sesame balls, etc.

**# 1881**

* It compares this passage with a similar one in AṅgSt (P 194b2f; D 186b7f; S 239b6f; F 167b4-6).

**1908 Das wäre, unter diesen Voraussetzungen, das Gleiche wie wenn man seine eigene Leiche essen würde, oder, etwas schief aber realistischer, den eigenen versehentlich abgehackten Finger.**

* This provides an analogy to illustrate the concept discussed in the text.

**1909 Aus der Sicht einer altruistisch orientierten Ethik wäre das Argument allerdings eher kontraproduktiv. Nach Fa-tsang 法藏 (643–712) jedenfalls besteht eine der vier Bedingungen, die erfüllt sein müssen, damit bei Fleischverzehr ein Vergehen vorliegt, darin, daß "es sich um das Fleisch eines anderen [Lebewesens] handelt; denn wenn man sein eigenes Fleisch ißt, gibt es kein echtes Vergehen" (T 40.1813: 636b20f: 二, 是他肉, 以食自肉無正犯故。). Nach Gyōnen 凝然 (1240–1321) kommt es auf das Motiv an. Ißt man sein eigenes Fleisch mit schlechten Gedanken (Selbsthaß?), ist das ein geringfügiges Unrecht. Ißt man es aus quälendem Hunger oder weil man unter einer Krankheit leidet, ist es kein Vergehen (T 62.2247: 177b5f: 食自肉者, 惡心 小罪。 飢急病 苦, 則無犯也。).**

* This discusses different perspectives on the ethics of consuming one's own flesh.
* It quotes opinions from Fa-tsang and Gyōnen.

**1910 Der japanische Sanron-Meister Chinkai (珍海, 1093–1152) bietet eine explizite Formulierung in diesem Sinne (T 70.2306: 741a16f):  
Alle Lebewesen haben die Buddha-Natur, alle werden Buddhas werden. Wie könnten wohl Buddha und Buddha einander essen?

* This presents a quote from the Japanese Sanron master Chinkai on the topic of consuming flesh.

**1911 AṅgSt P 159b7–163b4 (D 152b4–156a5; S 191b5–196b5; F 121b7–126b6). In AṅgSc (525c14-28) haben nur ganz wenige der über zwei Dutzend Abschnitte dieses Textstückes eine Entsprechung.**

* This refers to another section of the text and notes that only a few sections have corresponding parts in the AṅgSc version.

**1912 AṅgSt P 160b6–161a1 (D 153b3f; S 193a3-5; F 123a3-6; vgl. THUBTEN SOEPA o.J.: 6f): Lebewesen, die früher als (/wie?) Katzen (*m*ārjāra-bhūta*) ständig auf Fleischverzehr erpicht waren und nicht an den tathāgata*garbha* [in allen Lebewesen] glaubten, die sind auch jetzt (d.h. im gegenwärtigen Leben) Dämonen, die die Gestalt von Katzen angenommen haben, auf Fleisch erpicht sind und [dafür] Lebewesen töten. Auch diejenigen Lebewesen in der Zukunft, die als schreckliche (*mi bzad pa* = **ghora* o.ä., vgl. auch AṅgSt P 161a7) Dämonen, welche die Gestalt von Katzen angenommen haben, andere Lebewesen töten und [ihr] Fleisch essen und sich von 
[der] tathāgata*garbha*[-Lehre] abkehren, sind keine anderen, [sondern eben diese gegenwärtigen] Dämonen, welche die Gestalt von Katzen angenommen haben, andere Lebewesen töten und [deren] Fleisch essen.**

* This presents a passage from the text discussing beings who were previously obsessed with meat consumption and did not believe in the tathāgata*garbha.
* It describes their current and future states as demons taking the form of cats.

**1913 Diese Eingrenzung der aufgeworfenen Problematik auf Fälle, die wegen der im Normalfall zumindest indirekt implizierten Tiertötung auch aus buddhistischer Sicht relevant sein könnten, macht gegenüber der etwas breiteren, z.T. wohl nichtbuddhistische (/jinistische) Anschauungen voraussetzenden Fächerung der Beispiele im MPM (Text I: B.5) den Eindruck einer Präzisierung, was, falls zutreffend, gut zur Annahme der zeitlichen Priorität des letzteren passen würde.**

* This discusses the narrowing of the problem discussed in the text to cases relevant from a Buddhist perspective due to implied animal killing.
* It suggests this might indicate the MPM (Text I: B.5) is older than the current text.




##  Summary of the Text

**# 1972**

* A translation of LaṅkS attributed to Dharmakṣema is mentioned in a Chinese catalog but not found.
* This translation might have been similar in length to Guṇabhadras's version (LaṅkSGu), which consists of four chapters.

**# 1931**

* The *Ch'u san-tsang chi chi* mentions a translation of LaṅkS attributed to Dharmakṣema, but later catalogs list it as well.
* TOKIWA adds the title *Sarva-buddha-pravacana-hṛdayam* to this name.

**# 1932**

* Guṇabhadras's translation of LaṅkS is divided into four chapters, with the section on vegetarianism appearing at the end of chapter 4.
* The theme of "prohibition of meat consumption" is also hinted at in the question catalog at the beginning of chapter 2 of LaṅkSGu.

**# 1933**

* Guṇabhadras's translation might be missing some text, especially from chapter 10 which consists only of verses.
* However, the theme "meat consumption" is not addressed in chapter 10.

**# 1934**

* Guṇabhadras's translation was considered difficult to understand by Chinese scholars.
* Guṇabhadra was a vegetarian from childhood until his death according to *Kao seng chuan*.

**# 1935**

* The text discusses the location of chapter 8 in the Tibetan and Chinese versions of LaṅkS.

**# 1936**

* Some verses are arranged differently in Śi's version compared to the Sanskrit text.
* Guṇabhadras's translation is missing verses 10 and 11, and verse 18 appears at the end of the chapter.

**# 1937**


* The section headings used in this part are the same as those used in Text III.

**# 1938**

* A summary of arguments against meat consumption in LaṅkS is provided by HARVEY and DIVANJI.
* Tao-hsüan, a Vinaya master, offers a ten-point list of arguments against meat consumption based on Guṇabhadras's translation.

**# 1939**


* The text discusses the importance of compassion as a motivation for vegetarianism in Mahāyāna Buddhism.
* It also cites examples from Manu and Mahabharata that suggest a connection between vegetarianism and Hinduism.

**# 1940**

* HAM emphasizes the central role of this argument in the development of vegetarianism within Mahāyāna Buddhism.

**# 1941**


* The text mentions the avoidance of onions, garlic, and leeks in some Buddhist traditions.
* It cites examples from later literature that support this practice.

**# 1942**

* The text discusses the negative evaluation of meat consumption in some Ājīvika circles.

**# 1943**


* Verses from LaṅkSN and LaṅkSGu are compared, highlighting their similarities and differences regarding vegetarianism.
* Manu also mentions the avoidance of onions, garlic, and leeks.

**# 1944**

* The text discusses the potential for deception in meat sales and measures taken to prevent it.


**# 1945**

* LaṅkSN and LaṅkSGu are compared regarding their arguments against meat consumption based on the concept of "uncleanliness."
* Tao-shih's list of arguments against meat consumption does not include those related to uncleanliness or health hazards.


**# 1946**

* The text discusses the argument that meat consumption is morally wrong because it involves consuming semen and blood, which are considered sacred substances in some Buddhist traditions.
* It also cites a Jaina commentator who uses this argument against meat consumption.

**# 1947**

* LaṅkSN and LaṅkSGu are compared regarding their arguments against meat consumption based on the concept of "uncleanliness."


**# 1948**

* The text discusses the importance of compassion as a motivation for vegetarianism in Mahāyāna Buddhism.
* It also cites examples from Manu and Mahabharata that suggest a connection between vegetarianism and Hinduism.





## Summary of the text

**# The Harmlessness of Vegetarians**

* Since they have no intention to harm creatures, even wild animals like tigers, wolves and lions are tame and submissive towards vegetarians.
* Bodhiruci interprets a story about kings who encountered a lioness in the wilderness as evidence for the harmlessness of vegetarians. These kings were vegetarian and because of their abstinence from meat consumption, the lioness did not attack them.

**# Arguments Against Meat Consumption**

* The text presents various arguments against meat consumption based on Buddhist scriptures:
    * **Compassion:** Eating meat involves killing sentient beings, which contradicts the principle of compassion (ahimsa).
    * **Karma:** Killing animals creates negative karma, hindering spiritual progress.
    * **Mental and Physical Consequences:** Meat consumption is said to lead to bad dreams, fear, anxiety, greed, and even physical ailments.

**# The Role of Greed and Desire**

* The text emphasizes the role of greed and desire in driving meat consumption. It argues that craving for the taste of meat can lead to harmful actions, including cannibalism.
* The story of Siṁhasaudāsa/Kalmāṣapāda(/Sutasoma) illustrates how greed for meat can corrupt individuals and lead them astray from the path of righteousness.

**# Social and Economic Implications**

* The text criticizes those who profit from the slaughter and sale of animals, arguing that they are equally responsible for the suffering caused by meat consumption.
* It highlights the interconnectedness between consumers, butchers, fishermen, hunters, and all those involved in the meat industry.




## Summary of Text

**# Laṅkāvatāra Sūtra (LaṅkSN) and its Commentaries on Meat Consumption:**

* **Conservative Position:** The Mahābhārata (Mhbh) states that only those who eat meat without ritual authorization or a scriptural basis will go to hell. This reflects a conservative stance that accepts meat consumption within ritual contexts.
* **LaṅkSN's Emphasis on Negative Consequences:** Unlike the Mhbh, LaṅkSN focuses primarily on the negative consequences of meat-eating, such as bad karma, rebirth in lower realms, and hindering spiritual progress.

**# Arguments Against Meat Consumption in LaṅkSN:**

* **Bodhisattva Ethics:** The text emphasizes that Bodhisattvas should refrain from eating meat due to their compassion (dayā) and understanding of the interconnectedness of all beings (sarvasatvaikaputrakasaṁjñā).
* **Karma and Rebirth:** Meat consumption is seen as creating negative karma, leading to unfavorable rebirths.

**# Specific Verses and Passages:**

* **Verses 8.12-15:** Highlight the suffering of animals slaughtered for meat and the resulting negative karma for the consumer.
* **Verses 8.16-21:** Emphasize the Bodhisattva's compassion and avoidance of meat due to their understanding of the interconnectedness of all beings.

**# Commentaries on LaṅkSN:**

* **LaṅkSGu (Guṇabhadras Commentary):** Largely agrees with LaṅkSN, but sometimes omits references to Bodhisattvas specifically abstaining from meat.
* **Bodhiruci's Commentary:** Offers additional insights into the reasons behind avoiding meat consumption, such as the suffering of animals and the development of negative thoughts in those who kill for food.

**# Historical Context:**

* The LaṅkSN reflects a shift towards vegetarianism within Mahayana Buddhism, emphasizing compassion and non-violence towards all beings.
* While earlier texts like the Mhbh allowed meat consumption under certain circumstances, the LaṅkSN promotes a more strict vegetarian ideal for Bodhisattvas.





## # 2108 LaṅkSN 245,15

* This heading appears three times in the text: 245,15; 248,15; and 250,10.

## # 2109 JmV 1.19

* The Sanskrit term *sarvabhūtātmabhūta* is translated as "identifying himself with every living creature."
* A Tibetan translation renders it as "for whom all beings are his own (i.e., his dependents)."

## # 2110 Dasav vs. 4.9

* The term *sarvabhūtātmabhūta* is translated as "to a man who identifies himself with all beings."
* A Cuṇṇi commentary explains the expression with the Golden Rule: "As I here dislike pain, so to all living beings: in this thought he inflicts no suffering on them."

## # 2111 Mhbh 12.254.29–32; 12.261.32; 13.114.5–7; 13.130.27f.
* This heading lists several verses from the Mahabharata that mention *sarvabhūtātmabhūta*.

## # 2112 Mhbh 13.105.27

* The verse states that those who have renounced desires, are non-violent towards all beings (both stationary and moving), and consider themselves to be one with all beings are destined for the realm of Brahma.


## # 2113 Tib. P ngu 167b8f

* This heading refers to a Tibetan translation of LaṅkSN 245,15.
* The translation is given as "[the Bodhisattva], who wants that [for him] all living beings become like himself (or are)."

## # 2114 LaṅkSŚi 623a27

* The commentary states: "He regards all living beings as equal to himself."

## # 2115 LaṅkSBo 562c2-8

* This heading refers to a passage in the *Laṅkāvatāra Sūtra*.
* It argues that one should not eat meat because all beings cherish their lives and fear death.

## # 2117 Dies liegt jedenfalls für Mhbh 13.114.7 nahe, da unmittelbar zuvor von der Person, die alle Wesen als ihr selbst gleich ansieht (*ātmopamaś ca bhūteṣ*u), die Rede ist.

* This heading suggests that the context of Mhbh 13.114.7 implies that *sarvabhūtātmabhūta* refers to someone who sees all beings as themselves because they know the metaphysical unity of the Great Self in all beings.

## # 2119 Vgl. z.B. MahSūAl XIV.30, wonach ein Bodhisattva beim Eintritt in die erste Bodhisattva-Stufe zugleich mit der Schau des alles in gleicher Weise durchdringenden *dharmadhātu* mit Bezug auf alle Lebewesen das ständige Bewußtsein, daß sie ihm selbst gleich sind (sarvasatve*ṣu sadâtmasamacittatām*), erlangt, m.a.W.: die "Sicht eines Großen Ich oder Selbst" (s. En. 2113).

* This heading refers to a passage in the *Mahāsūtralamkāra*.
* It states that when a Bodhisattva enters the first stage of Bodhisattvahood, they attain the constant awareness that all beings are equal to themselves through the contemplation of the *dharmadhātu*, which pervades everything equally.

## # 2120 Eines der vier Wissen (*jðāna*) eines Buddha ist das aus der Vervollkommnung des entsprechenden Bewußtseins der Bodhisattvas (s. En. 2119) hervorgegangene "Gleichheitswissen" (*samatājðāna*): vgl. MahSūAl IX.70. Vgl. NAKAMURA 2016: 99f; 125f; 324f.

* This heading refers to the *Mahāsūtralamkāra*.
* It mentions that one of the four knowledges (*jñāna*) of a Buddha is the "Equality Knowledge" (*samatājñāna*), which arises from the perfection of the corresponding consciousness of Bodhisattvas.

## # 2128 Vgl. NAMAI 1993: 307 (I.2.0 u. I.2.1). ANDŌ 1992: 108f vertritt demgegenüber unter Berücksichtigung der jinistischen Versionen der SaudāsaSage die These, daß es ursprünglich zwei unabhängige Erzählstoffe gegeben haben könnte: eine Geschichte vom Menschenfresser Saudāsa (wie sie vor allem in den Jaina-Versionen vorliegt: s. auch HAMM 1951) und eine von der Bekehrung des Menschenfressers Kalmāṣapāda durch Sutasomas heroisches Einhalten seines Versprechens (in welche dann einige buddhistische Versionen die Saudāsa-Sage eingearbeitet hätten), und daß LaṅkSGu eher auf die ursprüngliche Saudāsa-Sage Bezug nehmen dürfte, insbesondere auf eine jinistische Fassung. Daß LaṅkSGu direkt oder indirekt auf eine jinistische Fassung zurückgegriffen habe, erscheine auch angesichts des strengen Fleischverzehrverbots im Jinismus plausibel. Ich möchte diese Möglichkeit keineswegs ausschließen, doch erscheinen mir einige Punkte problematisch. Erstens läßt sich die gleiche selektive Verwendung von Legendenmaterial im LaṅkS auch im Falle der Śibi- und der Kalmāṣapāda-Sage beobachten. Zweitens ist die von ANDŌ angesetzte Chronologie der Jaina-Versionen nicht gesichert. Er datiert Vimalasūris *Paumacariya* (mit JACOBI) ins 3. Jh. und Saṅghadāsas Vasudevahiṇḍi ins 6. Jh. (ANDŌ1992: 99 u. 102). Nach VON HINÜBER 2001a: \# 52 ist jedoch das *Paumacariya* jünger als die (wohl vor 450 verfaßte) Vasudevahiṇḍi und vielleicht im Jahre 473 n.Chr. entstanden,1 also später als LaṅkSGu. Eine vegetarische Bearbeitung des Saudāsa-Stoffes findet sich aber erstmals in der Version des *Paumacariya* (22.72–95, bes. 81–89), nicht in der der *Vasudevahiṇḍi*, und übrigens auch nicht in der Kurzfassung in Haribhadras (ca. 700–770 [BALBIR 1993: 93]) *Āvaśyaka-ṭīkā* (vgl. auch En. 2162). Ferner enden auch die Jaina-Versionen mit der *Bekehrung* des Menschenfressers (Paumac 22.90: *sodāso sāvao jāo*; Haribhadra [HAMM 1951: 72]: pavajjā. ... saṁ*buddho*; Raviṣeṇa: HAMM 1951: 70,1f). Lediglich in der (kaum jinistisch gefärbten) Fassung der *Vasudevahi*ṇḍi wird er, ganz im Sinne der Heldensagen, von Vasudeva getötet. Diese Version nennt aber den Menschenfresser (im Gegensatz zum *Paumacariu*) nicht Siṁha-Soyāsa, enthält auch keinen griffigen Ansatzpunkt für diese Benennung, und kommt deswegen als Quelle für LaṅkSGu kaum in Frage.

1 Auch L. ALSDORF vertrat (nach telef. Auskunft v. Klaus BRUHN) nachdrücklich eine spätere Datierung des *Paumacariya* als JACOBI.

* This heading discusses the possible sources of the Saudāsa story in LaṅkSGu, comparing it to Jain versions and noting the chronology issues involved.

## # 2140 Zu diesem Namen und seiner Deutung in buddhistischen Texten vgl. ENSINK 1970-75: 43f. Die vedische Bedeutung "der den Soma ausgepreßt hat" wird im buddhistischen Kontext obsolet, so daß der Name dort anders verstanden worden ist. Während JmV 217,6 "Sohn [so lieblich anzuschauen] wie der Mond" (*somapriyadarśanasya sutasya*; MEILAND 2009: II 328: Somavat priya°) von *suta* = "Sohn" ausgeht, belegt die Pāli-Tradition eine Auffassung von *suta* im Sinne von *śruta*, z.B. Jā V 177,16f sutavitt(ak)o savanasīlo ... tena Sutasomo ..., was ENSINK (ibid. 44) mit "He considered learning as wealth ..." wiedergibt. Wie °*soma* zu dieser Bedeutung kommen soll, ist mir unklar, und Gleiches gilt auch für die Erklärung von -*vitta(ka)* im Sinne von "devoted to" (DP s.v. jūtavittaka, dāna*vittaka*; vgl. AN-ṭ (CSCD Ekakanipāta-Ṭ \# 231, ad Mp I 319,5f): *sutavittako ti dhammassavanapiyo*). Eher nachvollziehbar wäre "berühmt für seine Gelehrsamkeit" (vgl. DP s.v. dāna*vittaka*, wo alternativ "famous for giving" angeboten wird, sowie AiGr II.2: 558: *vitta* "berühmt" u. Pāṇ 8.2.58), wenn man von einer Metapher "Mond" = "Leuchte" (der Gelehrsamkeit) ausgehen darf.

* This heading discusses the meaning of the name Sutasoma and its interpretation in Buddhist texts.

## Summary of Text

**#  T 2141**

* This section discusses the Buddhist perspective on vegetarianism. It argues that eating meat leads to suffering in future lives due to the killing involved.

**# T 2142-2146**

* These sections discuss textual parallels between the *Laṅkāvatāra Sūtra* and other Buddhist texts, particularly regarding the story of Saudāsa/Kalmāṣapāda.
* The text mentions Śikṣānanda's translation of the Laṅkāvatāra Sūtra and its agreement with a passage in T 3.164.

**# T 2147-2151**

* These sections discuss the karmic consequences of meat consumption, highlighting the belief that it leads to rebirth in hell realms.
* The text mentions the story of Saudāsa/Kalmāṣapāda as an example of someone who was reborn in a lower realm due to his meat-eating habits.

**# T 2152-2157**

* These sections explore the Buddhist concept of karma and its relation to diet.
* The text emphasizes that eating meat creates negative karma, leading to suffering in future lives.
* It also mentions the story of Saudāsa/Kalmāṣapāda's conversion to vegetarianism after experiencing the consequences of his past actions.

**# T 2158-2160**

* These sections discuss Jain perspectives on meat consumption, citing passages from Jain scriptures that condemn the practice.
* The text highlights the Jain emphasis on non-violence towards all living beings, including animals.

**# T 2161-2176**

* These sections delve into the historical and textual context of the Saudāsa/Kalmāṣapāda story.
* They discuss variations in the story across different Buddhist and Jain traditions, noting differences in the character's name and motivations.
* The text also examines the dating and authorship of various versions of the story, highlighting the challenges of reconstructing its origins.

## Summary of Text

**# Śāntideva and Meat-Eating**

* This section discusses the relationship between Śāntideva, a Buddhist scholar, and his stance on meat consumption.
* It mentions various sources that cite Śāntideva's views, including the *Śikṣāsamuccaya*, a collection of teachings attributed to him.
* The text analyzes passages from these sources, highlighting Śāntideva's apparent disapproval of meat-eating and its incompatibility with Buddhist principles like compassion (*maitrī*)

**#  The Vinaya on Meat Consumption**


* This section focuses on the rules regarding meat consumption in various Buddhist monastic codes (Vinayas).
* It cites specific passages from different Vinayas, including those of the Mahāsāṅghika, Mūlasarvāstivāda, and Theravāda schools.
* The text discusses the concept of a "legalizer" (*kappiyakāra*), who could authorize monks to consume meat under certain circumstances.

**#  The Case of the Deer Carcass**

* This section analyzes a specific episode found in several Vinayas, involving monks finding the carcass of a deer.
* It examines how different Vinayas describe the monks' actions and whether they required a "legalizer" to consume the meat.
* The text highlights variations in the narratives and discusses their implications for understanding monastic rules on meat consumption.

**#  Further Considerations**


* This section explores additional aspects of Buddhist teachings related to meat consumption, including:
    * The concept of *ahiṁsā* (non-violence) and its application to animals.
    * The role of intentionality in determining the ethical implications of consuming meat.
    * The historical context of Buddhist views on meat-eating.

## # Vinaya Texts on the Consumption of Meat

* This section examines various Vinaya texts regarding the consumption of meat, specifically focusing on situations where it is permissible or prohibited for monks and nuns.
* The discussion explores different Buddhist schools' perspectives on the matter, highlighting variations in their interpretations and practices.

**# The Case of Beutefleisch (Tiger, Lion, etc.)**

* Vinaya texts generally prohibit monks from consuming meat obtained by killing. 
* However, there are exceptions for meat found already dead, such as the remains left behind by a predator like a tiger or lion.
* This exception is based on the principle that the animal has already been killed and the monk is not directly responsible for its death.

**# Specific Examples:**

* Vin III 58,31-38: Monks encounter leftover meat from a lion kill and are initially hesitant to consume it. However, the Buddha clarifies that there is no transgression in eating such remains.
* Similar allowances are made for the remains of tigers, leopards, hyenas, and wolves.

**# Other Exceptions:**

* VinMā 468b27–c5:  It's permissible to consume meat dropped by a bird of prey, as the monk is not responsible for its death.
* Sp 379,29f: Meat found abandoned and discarded can be taken and consumed.

**# Restrictions:**


* Sp 373,7-9: Monks should avoid interfering with a predator actively feeding on its kill, as this could lead to conflict.
* Sp 373,10f: If a predator has already started eating its prey, it's permissible to take the meat once the animal has moved away.

**# The Concept of "Pārājika" Offenses:**

* VinSa (*Vinītaka*) 430b7-14 u. 431a14-19 ≈ VinMūUttt P phe 4a2-4 (D pa 3a4f; S na 4b4-7): Taking non-given property, including meat, is considered a "pārājika" offense, which leads to expulsion from the monastic order.
* However, there are nuances within this rule.

**# The Case of Mice Stealing Food:**

* VinSa (*Vinītaka*) 431a14-19 ≈ VinMūUttt P phe 5b5–6a2 (D pa 4b5–5a1; S na 7a1-6; VinSaMtṛ 588a4-9): A monk who destroys a mouse nest and takes the stolen food is not committing a "pārājika" offense, but it is discouraged by the Buddha.

**# The Importance of Context:**

* These texts highlight the importance of considering context when evaluating actions related to meat consumption.
* Factors such as the animal's death, the monk's intention, and potential harm to others are taken into account.

**# Variations Among Buddhist Schools:**


* Different schools of Buddhism may have slightly different interpretations and practices regarding meat consumption.
* This section explores these variations, providing examples from various Vinaya texts.

**# 2301 So WALDSCHMIDT 1944: 73 für Tobi in seiner Zusammenfassung der chinesischen Version der obigen Kṣ*udrakavastu*-Stelle, aber ohne Berücksichtigung der tibetischen Übersetzung. Dafür, daß auch tib. '*ol ba*' für die Eule verwendet werden kann, ließe sich m.W. allenfalls eine Stelle aus der *Māṇavikā* des Uttaragrantha des VinMū anführen (VinMūUttt P phe 217a1f; S na 326a7f; D pa 225b2f). Mit '*ol ba'i sgro* müssen hier Eulenflügelfedern gemeint sein. Hierfür spricht nicht nur die Parallele in VinSa (*Māṇavikā*) 406b11 (著角鴟翅衣, d.h. "Tragen eines Gewandes aus Eulenflügelfedern"; zu 角鴟 s. HCD 10: 1354a: 鴟鸺的别名; ChED: 鴟鸺 = owl), sondern auch VinSū 6.27 (keśamaya-nāgnya-ulūkapakkha*tva-*samādāna*m) und vor allem das in VinSūSvt D, Bd. zu, 123a7–b2 überlieferte Zitat der *Māṇavikā*-Stelle (*Bram* ze'i bu mos zhus pa las ...), wo es eindeutig "[Gewand] aus Eulen[federn]" (*bya 'ug pa pa*) heißt. Dies wird zusätzlich durch ein entsprechendes Verbot im Cīvara*kkhandha* des Theravāda-*Vinaya* gestützt (Vin I 305,33: vāakambalaṁ *pi dhārenti* vāakambalaṁ pi ... ulūka*pakkhikaṁ* pi ...; vgl. auch I 295,32f u. DN III 41,24f; BOLLÉE 1971: 84f). Ob an der *Māṇavikā*-Stelle '*ol ba*' tatsächlich die Eule bezeichnen soll oder ob eine Textstörung zugrundeliegt (*alika* statt *ulūka*??) oder vielleicht gar in der Vorlage nicht *ulūka* gestanden hat, sondern *kulāla*, das (nach PW auf der Basis von Hemacandra) auch die Bedeutung "Eule" haben kann und von den Übersetzern mit kulala/*kurara* (s. § e–i) verwechselt worden wäre (??), vermag ich nicht zu entscheiden.**

* This section discusses the use of the Tibetan word "*ol ba*" to refer to an owl.
* It cites various sources and analyzes the possible meanings and origins of this term.

## #2333

* MN I 364,28-30: *seyyathā pi … gijjho vā kaṅ*ko (v.l. kāko!) *vā kulalo vā* maṁsapesiṁ ādāya uḍḍayeyya, tam enaṁ gijjhā pi kaṅkā (/kākā) *pi kulalā pi* anupatitvā anupatitvā vitaccheyyuṁ …; zu kaṅka s. En. 618. In der Vorlage von MĀc hat offensichtlich *kāka* "Krähe" gestanden, und auch das einem vergleichbaren Kontext zugehörige Fragment SHT IV 30 f R5 spricht von Krähen (kāka), *kulala*s und Geiern. Vgl. auch Jā 330.2, wo der angegriffene Vogel unbezeichnet bleibt und die angreifenden Vögel *kulala*s sind, während in der vorangehenden Prosa (Jā III 100,15-21) u. einer Parallele dazu (Jā III 378,16-22) der Vogel, der sich das Stück Fleisch auf dem Fleischmarkt (*sūnāpaṇ*e) schnappt, ein sena (= *śyena*) ist und die verfolgenden Vögel (*saku*ṇā) entweder gar nicht oder (378,17) als "Geier usw." (*gijjhādayo*) spezifiziert werden. In der von R.O. FRANKE1 angeführten Mhbh-Parallele2 (ähnlich Utt 14.46) ist der Vogel, der das Fleischstück (*āmiṣ*a) erbeutet hat, ein *kurara*, u. die Vögel, die ihm das Fleischstück abjagen wollen und kurz davor sind, ihn zu töten – wovor er sich nur retten dadurch retten kann, daß er das Fleischstück losläßt –, werden nicht spezifiziert, könnten aber ebenfalls *kurara*s sein (*sāmiṣaṁ* kuraraṁ dṛṣṭvā vadhyamānaṁ nirāmiṣaiḥ | āmiṣasya parityāgāt kuraraḥ *sukham* edhate ||).

1 FRANKE 1906: 345 = 1978: 372; vgl. ANĀLAYO 2016a: 225 Anm. 31.
2 Am Ende von Mhbh 12.171, in der krit. Ausgabe Bd. 15, S. 982 [krit. Apparat] *475, Z. 3f.

## Brahminy Kite (Haliastur indus)

* **Diet:**
    * Primarily feeds on fish (dead or near the surface, stranded by receding floods).
    * Also eats garbage thrown overboard from ships in harbors.
    * Rarely scavenges carrion alongside vultures.

* **Food Sources and Hunting Behavior:**
    * Prefers garbage and offal over sharing large animal carcasses with vultures.
    * Sometimes steals food from other birds, like crows or even larger raptors.

## General Behaviour and Social Habits

* **Roosting:**
    * Outside of breeding season, Brahminy kites roost communally in trees, sometimes forming groups of hundreds.

* **Social Behavior:**

    * Generally solitary but can be seen in pairs during the breeding season. 


## Cultural Significance

* **Buddhist Texts:**

    * Mentioned in several Buddhist texts, often as an example of a creature that eats meat.
    * Used to illustrate the cycle of karma and rebirth, particularly the consequences of harming other beings.

* **Chinese Translations:**

    * The Brahminy kite is mentioned in some Chinese Buddhist translations attributed to An Shih-kao, but the authenticity of these texts is debated by scholars.

* **Symbolism:**
    * In some contexts, the Brahminy kite may symbolize greed or opportunism due to its scavenging habits.




## Text Summary

**2448**

* The author disagrees with Greene's interpretation of the omission of two characters in one version of a text.
* They argue that adding these characters later, perhaps due to Liang Wu-ti's promotion of vegetarianism, is plausible.
* Existing Vinaya codices may not have been changed but rather interpreted as steps towards a complete ban on meat consumption based on the Mahāparinirvāṇa-*mahāsūtra* and the La*ṅkāvatārasūtra*.

**2449**

* The first rule for monks prohibiting killing living beings does not mention meat consumption.

**2450**

* A text attributed to Mahāprajāpatī, dated before 374 CE, suggests that ascetics living with nuns could be tempted by sensual pleasures, including eating meat.
* This implies that the appeal of meat consumption for monks goes beyond mere taste and includes its sexually stimulating effects.

**2451-2453**

* T 1.20: 261a7-9 prohibits killing humans and cows.
* T 1.20: 260c23f discourages killing animals.
* T 1.20: 260c29 uses the term "bù hǎo" (not good) regarding killing animals, suggesting a preference against it.

**2454-2455**

* Schmithausen and Maithrimurthi discuss the prohibition of meat consumption in Buddhism.
* T 1.20: 260c8-11 lists various prohibited actions, including killing, stealing, adultery, lying, drinking alcohol, and sacrificing to evil spirits.
* The text emphasizes that cooking and killing animals for sacrifice is a severe transgression against Heaven's benevolence.

**2456-2457**

* The idea that gods/heavenly beings don't eat meat is also found in Indian Buddhism.
* T 55.2145: 17c8 mentions this concept.

**2458**

* Iwamoto provides appendices with references to texts mentioning Sumati, a figure associated with the story of a king who offers food to ascetics.

**2459-2460**


* T 2.129 and T 2.130 describe a story where a king invites ascetics to a meal.
* The focus is on the guests' inadequate clothing, not their nudity.
* Sumāgadhā criticizes the Brahman guests for their unattractive appearance in T 2.128A.

**2461-2462**

* T 2.128A mentions that the Brahman guests eat pork soup and thrice-fermented wine.
* This aligns with other versions of the story, but Sumāgadhā doesn't object to the food itself, only their revealing attire.
* The consumption of pork and alcohol is prohibited in some late Vedic texts and Dharma literature.

**2463-2464**


* T 2.128A criticizes the guests for indulging in delicious food without shame, comparing them to animals.
* T 2.128A also accuses the family of adhering to non-Buddhist gods and killing living beings for bloody food.

**2465**

* The terms "saunika" and "aurabhrika" are discussed in relation to people involved in the meat trade.
* They likely refer to individuals who raise, sell, or slaughter animals for consumption.


**2466-2468**

* The term "saṁstava" is debated in the context of a Buddhist text.
* Some manuscripts suggest it refers to butchers and slaughterers, while others use different terms.
* The meaning of "魁膾" (kuí kuài) is discussed, with possibilities including executioner, headsman, or butcher.

## 2469 Siehe En. 2465.

* **2470**  Die meisten Handschriften (s. En. 2471) lesen *māṁsikān* oder *mānsikān*, wofür BHSD 428a die Bedeutung "*butcher*, or *meat dealer*" ansetzt (vgl. auch ASBh 66,23: *māṁsikādīnāṁ*; Tj P shi 59b3: *sha la sogs pa 'dod pa rnams*). Tib. (Kj D mdo ja 104a4f) *nya pa* = "Fischer" verweist aber auf *mātsika*, was nach WATANABE 1975: 251 Anm. 26 von der Gilgit-Hs. bestätigt wird. Vgl. auch die chinesischen Versionen in En. 2472 u. En. 2473. Eine Verderbnis *mātsika* > mānsika > māṁ*sika* ist naheliegend. Zu mātsika = *nya pa* vgl. auch AKBh-I I: 
292. Auch an der AS-Stelle in LI 2013: 251,18 ist mit Tib. *nya pa* und Chin. 捕 魚 gewiß *mātsikānāṁ* zu lesen (vgl. BAYER 2010: 223,2 [lies *mātsik*a-]).

### # 2515 Sv 50,20–51,29, etc. (vier *suttanikkhepa*s): s. VON HINÜBER 1996: 115
(\# 230); 2013/14(2015): 372f; BrEncB 423f; SHIMODA 2000: 329f u. 337 Anm. 7.

* This footnote refers to several sources discussing the *suttanikkhepa*s, which are a type of Buddhist text.

### # 2516 MPM \# 169. Vgl. hierzu SHIMODA 2000. 

* This footnote points to a specific passage in the Madhyamaka-Prajñāpāramitā (MPM) and suggests consulting Shimoda's work for further information.

### # 2517 **[1.]** So mit CPD I 97a s.v. att'-*ajjhāsaya*, wo neben der substantivischen Bedeutung ("m. one's own determination or wish") unter ausdrücklichem Bezug auf die in En. 2515 angegebene Stelle eine adjektivische Verwendung ("mfn., due to ~") angesetzt wird. Eine solche liegt auch formal unzweideutig bei Pā. *pucchāvasiko* und aṭṭ*huppattiko* (sc. *nikkhepo*) vor. Diese Auffassung wird auch in der *Ṭīkā* (z.B.DN-ṭ I 73) vertreten, sofern man *nikkhepa* (naheliegenderweise) als Nomen actionis (nikkhipanaṃ *nikkhepo*) oder allenfalls im Sinne des Produktes dieser Handlung (d.h. der jeweiligen Lehrrede selbst: 
nikkhipīyatīti vā nikkhepo, suttaṃ *yeva nikkhepo*) auffaßt: "*attajjhāsaya* [bedeutet] ʻdie eigene Intention'; [im vorliegenden Kontext jedoch] ist *attajjhāsayo* [als ein Bahuvrīhi zu verstehen, dergestalt, daß] die [eigene Intention] ihm (sc. 

dem *nikkhepa*) zukommt, nämlich als Ursache für die Verkündigung der betreffenden Lehrrede" (attano ajjhāsayo attajjhāsayo, so assa atthi suttadesanā-*kāraṇabhūto ti attajjhāsayo*). Eine substantivische Auffassung von *attajjhāsayo* ließe sich, wie die *Ṭīkā* (wohl zu Recht) deutlich macht, nur aufrechterhalten, wenn man *nikkhepa* im Sinne von "dasjenige, wodurch [veranlaßt die betreffende Lehrrede] niedergelegt (d.h. verkündet) wird" (nikkhipīyati suttaṃ *etenā* ti suttanikkhepo) auffaßt. Eine solche Auffassung führt aber bei den Bestimmungen pucchā*vasika* und *aṭṭhuppattika* zu extrem gezwungenen Erklärungen. 

[2.] Im MPM sind auf Sanskrit leider nur der zweite und der dritte Terminus erhalten, u.zw. in der Form *parādhyāśay*ā und p*ṛcchāvaś*ā (s. En. 2520). 

YUYAMA (1981: 22f, Anm. 11 u. 12) sieht darin Pluralformen (°ā = Sandhi für °āḥ) und vermutet eine Fehlschreibung für Sg. *parādhyāśayo* und p*ṛcchāvaśo* (vgl. eṣa *parādhyāśayā nāma* in SF 13.7), die er in seiner Übersetzung 
(1981: 25: "considerateness *(adhyāśaya)* to others", "force of questions (p*ṛcchāvaśa)*") substantivisch interpretiert. Dagegen scheint mir allerdings zu sprechen, daß auch im MPM (\# 169) diese Ausdrücke Bestimmungen eines substantivischen Begriffs (*chos kyi sna grangs*) sein dürften und folglich analog zu der Pāli-Parallele adjektivisch zu interpretieren wären. Falls *chos kyi* sna grangs für *dharma*paryāya* (vgl. SHIMODA 2000: 330) steht, wären in der Tat wie im Pāli maskuline Formen zu erwarten. Alternativ erwägt YUYAMA
(1981: 23 Anm. 12), °*vaśā (nāma)* auf °*vaśān (nāma)* (Sandhi für °*vaśāt (nāma)*) 
zurückzuführen: "by means of, according to questions", was inhaltlich gut passen würde und auch auf das vorangehende Stichwort anwendbar wäre: eṣa parādhyāśayā⟨n⟩ *nāma* = "dies ist [die Belehrungsweise] aufgrund der Intention (/des Wunsches) eines anderen (bzw. anderer Personen)".

### # 2523 In MPM \# 182,9-14 wird die Kategorie der p*ṛcchāvaśa*-Belehrungen sogar ausdrücklich dahingehend definiert, daß sie durch Fragen des der gleichen Sippe wie Mahākāśyapa angehörenden Bodhisatva veranlaßt sind. In diesem Textstück geht es aber offenbar nicht um eine allgemeine Definition der vier Belehrungsweisen, sondern um ihre spezielle Ausdeutung im Hinblick auf das Mahāpari*nirvāṇa-mahāsutra* (wobei die vierte Belehrungsweise, sc. *arthotpatti*vaśa*, gar nicht erwähnt wird). Selbst wenn sicher wäre, daß dieses Textstück zum älteren Textbestand gehört (und nicht etwa ein Nachtrag ist), würde dies nicht zwingend implizieren, daß es mit seiner Aussage zu p*ṛcchāvaśa* konkret auf den Fleischverzehr-Abschnitt Bezug nimmt, da die besagte Person im MPM ja allenthalben als Fragesteller auftritt.

### # 2535 NANJŌ Bun'yū 南條文雄 and IZUMI Hōkei 泉芳璟: Bonbun Nyū-*ryōga-kyō:* hōyaku 梵文 入楞伽經 邦譯, Kyoto: Nanjo Sensei koki kinen shukuga-kai 1927.

* This footnote cites the publication details of another Japanese translation of the Lankāvatāra Sūtra.

### # 2537 Dwight GODDARD, *Self-Realization of Noble Wisdom: A version Based on* Dr. Suzuki's translation of the Laṅkāvatārasūtra (Thetford 1932); nachgedruckt als D.T. Suzuki tr., Dwight GODDARD, compiled and edited: The Lankavatara Sutra: An Epitomized Version (Varanasi: Pilgrims Publishing 2005).

### # 2538 William BAGLEY: *A Re-translation of the Eighth Chapter of the Lankavatara* Sutra and Commentary. Published in PDF with permission of the copyright holder, William Bagley, by Shabkar.Org - Amsterdam.
* This footnote cites the author and publication details of a re-translation of the eighth chapter of the Lankāvatāra Sūtra and its commentary.

### # 2539 Ein paar Beispiele, die sich leicht um ein Vielfaches vermehren ließen, mögen genügen: S. 248,18-20 (B.1.1; Na 245,11) übersetzt GOLZIO die Phrase nāsty asau kaścit sattvaḥ sulabha*rūpo (yo ...)* mit "... gibt es kein einziges Wesen, das, wenn es die Form eines lebenden Wesens angenommen hat, ...", 
was offenkundig nur eine Verdeutschung von SUZUKIs (1932: 212,15-17) Wiedergabe ist ("there is not one living being that, having assumed the form of a living being, ..."), nicht aber eine Übersetzung des Originals, das gewiß im Sinne von "es gibt kein solches Lebewesen, das leicht zu finden wäre, das ..." zu verstehen ist (vgl. auch Tib [P ngu 167b7] *sems can de ni rnyed par sla ba'i* rnam pa gang yang med do). S. 249,23f (B.4.2; Na 246,15) lautet die Übersetzung: "... indem er denkt: ‹Das sind Todeshändler, sie werden auch mich töten.›", ganz wie bei SUZUKI (1932: 213,12f): "... thinking, 'They are deathdealers, they will even kill me.'"); Skt. mara*ṇaprāptāś caike bhavanty asmān* api mārayiṣ*yanti* bedeutet aber gewiß: "... und einige [Hunde] sind nahe daran, 
[vor lauter Angst] zu sterben, in dem Gedanken: 'Die werden auch uns töten!'" (vgl. Tib [P ngu 168a8] la la ni bdag cag kyang bsad du 'ong ngo zhes shi la thug par 'gyur ro). Auf S. 250,8 (B.6.1; Na 247,9) wird k*ṛpātmano*, 
wiederum nach SUZUKI (213,30) "whose nature is holy", mit "dessen Natur heilig ist" übersetzt, anstatt - wie S. 248,15 (B.0; Na 245,9) - mit "dessen Wesen Mitleid ist" (SUZUKI 212,13f: "whose nature is compassion"). S. 254,18 (C.1; Na 252,16) hat die deutsche Übersetzung "aus Stolz" anstelle von Skt. 

mūlya*hetor*, was ganz offensichtlich auf SUZUKIs (217,17) "force of pride" beruht. Es ist zu bezweifeln, ob diese Interpretation zutrifft.

[2.]  Es scheint, dass GOLZIO die Übersetzung von SUZUKI in einigen Punkten unkritisch übernimmt und dabei eigene Fehlinterpretationen produziert.

### # 2540 You have provided a detailed analysis of the footnotes and their content. It seems like you are working on a complex text with many layers of interpretation and translation. Is there anything specific you would like to know about these footnotes or the text itself?

## # Gänzlich anders jedoch die Wiedergabe von *sarvabhūtātmabhūtatām* upagantu*kāmānām* (... *bodhisattvānām ...*) in **B.13**.3b (Na 250,10) :

-  Wiedergabe des Ausdrucks in B.1.2 (Na 245,13-16).
-  Interpretation von °bhūtatām upa√gam als "entrinnen [aus dem Saṁsāra]".


## # Dafür scheint zumindest die Wiedergabe des Ausdrucks in B.1.2 (Na 245,13-16) zu sprechen.

-  Wiedergabe des Ausdrucks in YU's Übersetzung.
-  Interpretation von *ātman* als das "Selbst" im Sinne des eigentlichen, ursprünglichen Wesens aller Lebewesen.

## # In C.2-3 (Na 253,3-7) versteht YU zwar (anders als GOLZIO und TAN: s. En. 2539 u. 2541) im Gegensatz zu SUZUKI den Ausdruck śākuntikaurabhraka-kaivartā*daya*ḥ korrekt

-  Problematische Punkte in YU's Übersetzung.
-  Identifikation des Agens (*mohapuruṣaih*) mit dem Subjekt des yad-Satzes.
-  Wiedergabe des Ausdrucks itara-mṛga-pakṣi-prāṇi-saṁbhūta-*māṁsāni*.

## # 2546 GOODMANs Übersetzung der im *Śikṣāsamuccaya* zitierten LaṅkS-Verse enthält leider neben kleineren Ungenauigkeiten auch einige gravierendere Mißverständnisse, insbesondere die folgenden:

-  GOODMAN's Wiedergabe von ŚikṣB 132,2f (= LaṅkS 8.4/E.4).
-  GOODMAN's Wiedergabe von ŚikṣB 132,8f (= LaṅkS 8.9/E.9).
-  GOODMAN's Übersetzung von ŚikṣB 133,13 (= LaṅkS 8.20cd/E.20cd).
-  GOODMAN's Übersetzung von ŚikṣB 134,1 (bhaiṣajyam iva āhāraṁ putramāṁsopamaṁ *puna*ḥ, ≈ LaṅkS 8.22ab/E.22ab).

## # 2548 Im Falle der tibetischen Übersetzung aus dem Sanskrit (LaṅkSt) lege ich die in Teil 3, Text III (Ed.), Vorbem. § 6.2.5 aufgeführten Textzeugen zugrunde

-  Auswahl von Repräsentanten der unterschiedlichen Überlieferungsstränge.
-  Stellenangaben beschränken sich normalerweise auf die jeweilige Stelle in P.
-  Varianten werden nur verzeichnet, wenn sie inhaltlich oder syntaktisch von Bedeutung sind.


## # 2549 Für das Verständnis von Guṇabhadras Version habe ich auch deren Übersetzung ins Tibetische (LaṅkSGt) herangezogen

-  Unsicherheit, ob die tibetische Übersetzung stets das Richtige trifft.

## # 2550 LaṅkV(Jbh) P ni 298a3–302a4; D ni 257b4–261a7; HAD. 515–523; ZT Bd. 69: 1439,9–1448,1. Jñānaśrībhadras Ausführungen behandeln das Thema Fleischverzehr und damit verbundene Fragen unabhängig vom Text des Sūtra

-  Bezugnahme auf den Text des Sūtra nur sporadisch indirekt.

## # 2551 LaṅkV(Jvj) P pi 303b7–307b6; D pi 262b3–265b4; ZT Bd. 70: 635,13–643,2.

-  Keine Angaben zum Inhalt.
