# 3086 Die Verwendung von *kṛta* mit Bezug auf Fleisch im Sinne von "[durch Tötung des Tieres] hergestellt (oder) zubereitet"*, fungiert als - , ist zu erwartenden *(a)kārita*. Ob °kṛ*taka° schlicht als Ausdrucksvariante statt °kṛta zu bewerten ist oder gegen alle Hss. zu °kṛta° emendiert werden sollte, muß ich dahingestellt sein lassen. Oder soll etwa mit der Verwendung von *(a)kṛtaka* statt *(a)kṛta* zusätzlich angedeutet werden, daß Fleisch (als Nahrungsmittel) grundsätzlich durch Tötung von Tieren hergestellt werden muß, d.h. daß es spontan, ohne fremdes, insbesondere menschliches Zutun verfügbares Fleisch überhaupt nicht gibt? Das wäre allerdings nur dann stimmig, wenn Fleisch von Tieren, die eines natürlichen Todes gestorben sind, nicht (als Nahrungsmittel) in Betracht gezogen wäre. 

1 Eine Ausnahme bildet YU's (2008/2013, ad Na 253,10) semantisch kaum haltbare Wiedergabe von akṛtam akāritam asaṁ*kalpitam* mit добыта, приготовлена и предложена [подающим её] (d.h. "erworben, vorbereitet und angeboten [von dem, der sie (= die Speise) darreicht]").

## Summary of the text:

**3087**

* This section discusses the use of *kārita* alongside *kṛta* in connection with bad actions.
* Often found in Jaina texts, but sometimes Buddhist, a third element is added indicating agreement or consent to the action.
* Examples are given from various scriptures illustrating this concept.


**3088**

* The term *saṁkalpita* has multiple interpretations and is translated differently by old and modern translators.
* One possibility is that *saṁkalpita* means "imagined" in the sense of "desired," "hoped for," or "[in advance deliberately or at least approvingly] factored in."

**3089**

* The majority of manuscripts read *anujānīyāṁ*. 
* NANJIO's reading, *anujānīy*aṁ, is not supported by any accessible manuscripts and is considered an irregular Gerundivum.
* Most translators do not object to NANJIO's reading and seem to understand it as an irregular Gerundivum.

**3090**

* This section examines the Tibetan translation of the passage (P ngu 170b7).
* It also analyzes Chinese translations by Bo and Śi, noting their interpretations of the three key terms.
*  Bodhiruci's interpretation is highlighted for its attempt to explicitly clarify the intention of the passage.
* Guṇabhadra's translation is discussed, emphasizing its inclusion of "fish" and its differences in wording compared to other versions.

## Summary of Text

**[4.1a]**

* The concept of "triply pure" meat in E.12 suggests the text primarily addresses ordained individuals' meat consumption.
* The term *ayācita* ("not requested") evokes alms recipients, linking to C's section on indirect responsibility for animal slaughter through meat consumption.
* A hypothetical objection from ordained individuals could be: "We don't buy meat! If we eat it, it's given by laypeople and is 'triply pure,' ensuring the animal wasn't killed specifically for us."

**[4.1b]**

* Traditional *trikoṭi* deems meat impure if the recipient knows or suspects the animal was killed for them.
* D.1(Gu) and E.12 introduce three new conditions, potentially stricter than *trikoṭi*, concluding that none reliably distinguish permissible from impermissible meat.
* These new conditions likely exclude "pure" meat entirely, though the text doesn't explicitly explain how.

**[4.2]**

* The three conditions for permissible meat in D.1(Gu) and E.12 are unique to these texts.
* A partial parallel exists in BauDhS 2.[10.]18.12, where a renunciant can eat a simple meal received without solicitation or from unselected houses.

**[4.3]**

* T 39.1789 offers two explanations for the three prohibited actions (codita, yācita, saṁkalpita) in D.1(Gu) and E.12.
* The first explanation connects *codita* to butchers inciting others to kill, *yācita* to hunters/fishermen catching animals for profit, and *saṁkalpita* to consumers' appetite triggered by seeing animal carcasses.

**[5.1]**

* D.1's Sanskrit version differs from Gu's version and E.12, suggesting a shift in perspective rather than intention.
* Key terms change (acoditam ayācitam a(saṁ)kalpitaṁ to akṛtam akāritam asaṁkalpitam).

**[5.2]**

* Mentioning "hearers" (*śrāvaka*) implies the text addresses all followers of Buddha's teachings, including monks/nuns and laypeople.
* The term "permissible" (*kalpya*) suggests a focus on ordained individuals, highlighting that even they are prohibited from meat consumption.

**[5.3]**




##  Summary

### # kṛta, *kārita* und saṁ*kalpita*:

* The terms *kṛta*, *kārita*, and *saṁkalpita* are likely referring to the actions of laypeople who provide food to ordained individuals.
* It is unlikely that ordained individuals themselves would kill animals for meat or actively arrange for their killing.

### # Parallels in Jain Texts:

* A parallel passage in a Jain text supports the interpretation of *asaṁkappiya* as "not intended for" at the time of preparation, rather than just not intended by the donor.
* This suggests that the three conditions (akṛta, akārita, asaṁkalpita) are meant to ensure that the meat was not specifically prepared for the ordained individual.

### # Differences between Jain and Buddhist Texts:

* While both texts share similar criteria for permissible meat, there is a key difference in their intention.
* The Jain text aims to restrict certain types of food, while the LaṅkS completely prohibits meat consumption.

### # Interpretation of D.1:

* The author suggests that at least one of the three conditions in D.1 must be interpreted more strictly than in the Jain source to account for the total prohibition of meat.
* This stricter interpretation could involve a broader definition of *kṛta* and *kārita*, encompassing any meat where the donor was involved in the animal's death, regardless of whether it was intended for ordained individuals.

### # Alternative Interpretations:

* The author considers two possible interpretations of D.1:
    * **[α]** There is no meat that meets all three conditions and is therefore permissible.
    * **[β]** There is no meat that meets all three conditions and is therefore permissible, even if it appears to be acceptable under a less strict interpretation.

### # Jñānavajra's Commentary:


* Jñānavajra's commentary on D.1 supports the interpretation of [β], stating that even meat that meets the three conditions is not permissible because it still indirectly harms living beings.
* He argues that consuming such meat contributes to the demand for meat and ultimately leads to animal slaughter.

## [7.1c] Drittens - und das ist von besonderem Interesse - geht es nach Jñānavajra in D.1 nicht darum, klarzustellen, daß Fleischverzehr nicht nur den Bodhisattvas, sondern allen "Hörern" des Buddha, insbesondere auch den traditionellen Ordinierten, verboten ist.

* Er konstruiert den Text vielmehr so, daß das den drei Bedingungen (akṛta usw.) entsprechende Fleisch den Śrāvakas, hier eindeutig im Sinne der traditionellen Hörer bzw. Ordinierten, erlaubt ist.
* Die Erklärung des Textes, daß auch der Verzehr solchen Fleisches unzulässig sei, kann sich daher nur auf die Bodhisattvas bzw. die "höheren Fahrzeuge" (die ja durchaus auch ordinierte Asketen sein können) beziehen, und entsprechend habe ich dies in meiner obigen Wiedergabe auch ergänzt.

* Dies wird von einer anderen Stelle (LaṅkVJvj P pi 306a6f; D pi 264b2; ZT 70: 640,6-8) ausdrücklich bestätigt: Auch das den Śrāvakas erlaubte in dreifacher Weise reine Fleisch usw. ist in den höheren Fahrzeugen verboten worden.

## [7.2] Eine ganz ähnliche Auffassung vertritt auch Jñānaśrībhadra.

* Auch bei ihm heißt es, daß für die gewöhnlichen Ordinierten dreifach reines Fleisch –
solches, für das sie (bzw. die Spender) weder eigenhändig ein Tier getötet noch die Tötung veranßt haben und auf das sie es auch nicht abgesehen hatten - , wenn es ihnen als Almosenspeise (*slong ba'i zas*, **bhaikṣa*?) gegeben wird, ohne daß sie darum gebeten oder es erwartet hätten, vollkommen rein wie Reisspeise und glückbringend wie eine Arznei sei.
* Den mitleidvollen Bodhisattvas hingegen sei auch solches [Fleisch] verboten.

* Insbesondere gelte dieses Verbot für Personen, die sich mit magisch-kräftigen Formeln auskennen (**mantrin*), da sie nur so die Lebewesen für sich gewinnen (*ārādhana*) und die Gottheiten gnädig stimmen (*abhipra*sādana*) können, oder weil ihnen andernfalls die fleischfressenden Dämonen *(*piśāca)* Hindernisse bereiten oder Schaden zufügen könnten.

* Eine Mitverantwortung des Almosenempfängers für etwaige indirekt mit der Gabe assoziierte Tötungshandlungen wird hier mit Gründen, die an die Tarka*jvālā* (s. St. § 130) erinnern, abgelehnt.

## [7.3] Gegen die in den indo-tibetischen Kommentaren vertretene Deutung wäre allerdings einzuwenden, daß sie den Optativ *anujānīyāṁ* in D.1 nicht gebührend berücksichtigt;

* denn dieser kann doch wohl nur bedeuten, daß es kein zulässiges Fleisch gibt, das der Buddha seinen Hörern/Schülern (śrā*vaka*), also auch den traditionell orientierten, erlauben könnte.
* Auch Jñānaśrībhadra bemerkt an einer Stelle immerhin, daß den Śrāvakas dreifach reines Fleisch, weil es schwer zu bekommen (*durlabha*) ist, zwar nicht verboten, aber doch auch nicht [ausdrücklich] erlaubt worden sei.

* Es kann in der Tat angesichts der unzweideutigen Darlegungen in den folgenden Textabschnitten (**D.2–5**) kaum bezweifelt werden, daß das LaṅkS selbst nicht nur jegliche den Śrāvakas angeblich erteilte Erlaubnis, Fleisch zu essen, bestreitet, sondern dieses Fehlen einer Erlaubnis auch durchaus im Sinne eines totalen Verbotes (vgl. D.3.2 u. D.4.3) versteht, und daß dieses totale Verbot keineswegs nur für die Bodhisattvas gedacht ist, sondern für alle Hörer/Schüler (*śrāvaka*) des Buddha, also auch für "mainstream"-Mönche und erst recht für Mahāyāna-Mönche, Gültigkeit haben soll (vgl. auch D.5.5).

* In einigen tibetischen Textzeugen fehlt im zweiten Teil des Satzes die Negation, wodurch der Sinn ins Gegenteil verkehrt wird.

## #3116

# Geisteshaltung des Wohlwollens

* Der Text beschreibt die Motivation hinter dem Verbot von Fleischkonsum für bestimmte Yogis.
* Ziel ist es, dass diese ihre spirituellen Praktiken erfolgreich durchführen können.
* Dies gilt insbesondere für Bodhisattvas, die die Vorstellung kultivieren sollen, dass alle Lebewesen ihnen so nahe stehen wie ihr einziger Sohn.

# Textkritische Anmerkungen

* Der Autor diskutiert verschiedene Lesarten des Textes und bevorzugt eine bestimmte Interpretation.
* Er vergleicht seine Übersetzung mit anderen Übersetzungen und kommentiert deren Stärken und Schwächen.
* Der Autor geht auf die Schwierigkeit der syntaktischen Analyse des Satzes ein und bietet eine eigene Interpretation an.

# Vergleich mit anderen Quellen

* Der Autor bezieht sich auf andere buddhistische Schriften, insbesondere das Vinaya und den MPM (Mahāprajñāpāramitāśāstra).
* Er vergleicht die Zahl der verbotenen Fleischarten in verschiedenen Quellen und versucht, Diskrepanzen zu erklären.
* Der Autor erwähnt verschiedene Interpretationen des Textes in anderen buddhistischen Traditionen.

# Ungeklärte Punkte

* Die genaue Bedeutung von "neun uddiśyakṛtāni" ist unklar und Gegenstand von Diskussionen.
* Die Herkunft der Zahl "fünf" in Guṇabhadras Übersetzung ist nicht eindeutig geklärt.
* Der Autor vermutet eine mögliche Textverderbnis in einigen Handschriften.

##  Summary of Text


### # 3 Bei der Erklärung in einem Kommentar ...

* This section discusses a possible late explanation attempt regarding the Śūraṁgama*sūtra*.
* It suggests that the explanation might be inappropriate in the context of the LaṅkS.

### # [4] Was den überlieferten Sanskrit-Text ...

* This section analyzes the Jaina commentator Samayadivākara's list of nine reasons for permissible meat consumption.
* It explores whether this list aligns with the ideas presented in MPM and LaṅkS.
* The text considers the possibility that the number "nine" arises from combining traditional concepts like *trikoṭi* (seen, heard, suspected) with other groupings.

### # 3127 Zur Unzulässigkeit von *uddiśyakṛta* s. auch LaṅkS ...

* This section discusses the prohibition of *uddiśyakṛta* (meat specifically prepared for a monk) in Buddhist Vinaya and Jain traditions.
* It notes that while Buddhism restricts this type of meat, Jainism prohibits all specially prepared food for monks.

### # 3128 Gu ist erheblich kürzer:

* This section focuses on the shorter version of Gu's text regarding permissible meat consumption.
* It highlights the problem with the number "five" mentioned in Gu's statement.

### # 3129 [1] Gu (514a8) wieder kürzer:

* This section compares Gu's concise statement about prohibiting ten types of meat to other versions.
* It notes that Śi also mentions a prohibition of ten meat types, contrasting it with the allowance of three types.

### # 3129 [2] Bo (563c15f u. 20f) stellt Fleisch ...

* This section analyzes Bo's distinction between meat from animals killed intentionally and those that died naturally.
* It emphasizes that the focus is on whether one witnessed or suspected the killing, not just the animal's cause of death.

### # 3129 [3] Im Falle von Gu und Śi gibt es ...

* This section suggests that Gu and Śi likely refer to the ten tabooed meat types mentioned in various Vinaya texts.
* It notes that Bo probably had these ten types in mind as well, based on his inclusion of material about socially taboo meats.

### # 3129 [4] Erhebliche Schwierigkeiten bereitet dem Verständnis ...

* This section discusses the challenges posed by the Tibetan text's rendering of a specific sentence.
* It explores various interpretations, including one that aligns with a Sogdian text's classification of meat based on the animal's cause of death.

### # 3129 [5] Die in der tib. Wiedergabe von D.4.2 ...

* This section examines Jñānavajra's view that consuming meat from naturally deceased animals is not problematic.
* It highlights Jñānavajra's reasoning that these animals are not harmed by the consumption of their flesh.





## Summary of Text Sections

**3133:** This section discusses the meaning of "hellfire" in relation to the Buddhist concept of rebirth in unfavorable realms.

* The Chinese translations (Bo and Śi) explicitly understand this as rebirth in undesirable places.
* CLEARY's translation of "hellfire" is mentioned but not necessarily endorsed.

**3134:** This section defines lacto-vegetarian food, contrasting it with problematic meat consumption.

* *Prākṛ*ta is used here in the neutral sense of "natural, normal, everyday," not in a derogatory sense as "vulgar."
* CLEARY's translation ("the food of primitive people") and TOKIWA's (1994: I 271,5) are criticized for misrepresenting the meaning.

**3135:** This section discusses Bo's interpretation of a passage about food restrictions.

* Bo seems to have struggled with this statement and interprets it as prohibiting even foods normally allowed for listeners if they are hoarded or received improperly.

**3136:** This section presents Gu's (514a10f) version of the statement about food restrictions, which is similar to DEMIÉVILLE (1940: 189) and TAKASAKI/HORIUCHI (2015: 302,4f).

* The Tathāgatas, the awakened ones, do not eat anything other than that, let alone fish and meat, and they do not teach others to do so.

**3137:** This section refers to En. 2859.

**3138:** This section discusses variations in the text of the LaṅkS regarding the listing of those who abstain from meat.

* The grammatically correct reading is found in Hss. {C} {D} Ry etc., but the reading in Hss. T1 {A} {B} may be older.

**3139:** This section explains that the passage about food restrictions refers to those who have attained the bliss of absorption (ting nge 'dzin gyi bde ba thob pa).

* LaṅkVJvj P 306b8 (D pi 265a1f; ZT 70: 641,8f) clarifies this.
* Similar statements are found in T 39.1791: 497b28 and A.2 with En. 2851.

**3140:** This section discusses the meaning of *āmiṣ*a, which refers to material food as opposed to "spiritual" nourishment from Dharma or bliss of absorption.

* Bo (564a8) states that those who eat the food of Dharma do not eat material food.
* CLEARY's translation ("... eat for truth, not for enjoyment") is deemed inaccurate.

**3141:** This section mentions a similar statement in the MPM: see Text I: B.8 and St. § 196.

**3142:** This section offers two possible translations of a phrase: "desire [and] search for ..." or "the desire and pursuit of ...".

**3143:** This section defines *upakara*ṇas as food (*bhojana*), clothing (*vastra*), and ornaments (ala*ṅkāra*) according to YBhBha 34,7f.

**3144:** This section refers to VETTER (1993: 66 with note 51) and ZACCHETTI (2005: 242 (\#\# 1.4 and 1.5)) for further information on *ceta*ḥ- and *prajñāvimukti*.

**3145:** This section notes En. 2852.

**3146:** This section explains that the future tense is used to express impossibility, citing BECHERT (1958: 64 (\# f)).

* Gu states: "Therefore I do not allow ..." (是故不聽 ...).

**3147:** This section discusses *anujðātavān asmi* and compares it to D.3.2 k*ṛtavān ... asmi* (see En. 3115).
* Tib. (P ngu 171b6) uses *ngas ... gnang ba* for *asmi*, which is preferred over *aham*.

**3148:** This section explains that Bo (564a14-18) has a different text passage than the LaṅkS, justifying why those seeking enlightenment should avoid garlic, onions, and alcohol.

**3149:** This section discusses discrepancies in the verse numbering between different versions of the Śikṣ.
* The Chinese translation (T 32.1638: 100b28–c6) only contains verses 4–6, 9, and 14–15.

**3150:** This section analyzes the arrangement of verses in different versions of the text.
* Gu places verses 1–3 at the beginning of the chapter, while Śi puts them after Mahāmati's prose speech.
* Bo follows the Sanskrit version but attributes the verses to the Buddha rather than Mahāmati.

**3151:** This section discusses variations in the text regarding the word *jinapuṅgava*.

* NANJIO uses *jinapuṅgavai*ḥ, but some manuscripts have *jinapuṅgava*ḥ.
* The nominative form is not constructible, and the reading is likely corrupted.

**3152:** This section analyzes the first line of verse 1 and its grammatical difficulties.

* NANJIO's text corresponds to most manuscripts but poses challenges in terms of syntax and meaning.

**[1.]**

* Huang translates *bhāṣ*adbhi*ḥ as a finite verb, but Cleary's translation is more accurate.
* Tokiwa attempts a meaningful translation by separating *jinapuṅgavai*ḥ and substantivizing it.

**[2.]**

* The author argues for the reading *jinapuṅgava* based on stronger textual support and grammatical considerations.
* The author also prefers the reading °*ṇḍuṁ* ca for Pāda a and *na bhakṣyaṁ peyam eva v*ā for Pāda b.

**[4.]**

* This section explores different interpretations of the Sanskrit word *ārya* in the context of the verse.
* The author analyzes various translations and textual variations, including Tibetan, Chinese, and other Sanskrit manuscripts.





##  Analysis of the Text

### # Mahāmati's Question

* The text begins with a question posed by Mahāmati. 
* She seeks to understand the advantages and disadvantages of eating meat versus abstaining from it.

### #  Guṇabhadra's and Śikṣānanda's Versions

* Guṇabhadra (Gu) and Śikṣānanda (Śi) both present slightly different versions of Mahāmati's question in their respective translations.
* Both versions emphasize the desire to know the karmic consequences of meat consumption and abstinence.

### # Parallels with Other Texts

* The phrasing used by Mahāmati echoes similar expressions found in other Buddhist texts, such as the *Mahābhārata*.

### #  The Buddha's Response

* The Buddha's response to Mahāmati's question is not fully provided in the text excerpt.
* However, it is implied that he will address the ethical and karmic implications of meat consumption.

### # Arguments Against Meat Consumption

* The text mentions arguments against eating meat, including:
    * It causes harm to sentient beings.
    * It involves consuming flesh mixed with impure substances.
    * It can lead to spiritual decline.

### #  Ascetic Practices

* The excerpt also touches upon ascetic practices recommended for yogis, such as avoiding the use of oil and sleeping on beds with holes.




## Summary of Text

**[2] Different Translations and Interpretations**

* Suzuki follows the Sanskrit text closely, while Cleary and Goodman don't translate *viddha* and suggest a bed of nails used for self-mortification.
* Bendall/Rouse paraphrases the Tibetan, while Yasui follows Chinese versions.
* Tokiwa suggests an emendation but notes its irregularity.

**[3] Analysing "śalyaviddha"**

* The text discusses whether to follow the Sanskrit *śalyaviddha* or the Tibetan *talpaviddha*.
* Most manuscripts support *śalya*, which is confirmed by the Śikṣāsamuccaya manuscript.
* An emendation to *talpe viddhe* would require a significant change and might not be accurate.

**[4] Understanding "śalyaviddha" in Context**

* The context suggests that *śalyaviddha* refers to beds with holes or openings, not beds used for self-mortification.
* The author speculates that *śalya* might refer to pegs used in bed construction based on the meaning of *śalya* as "peg".
* Jñānavajra's paraphrase suggests that the concern is about frightening small creatures living in the holes of the bed.

**[3174]  "Sattva" as Small Creatures**

* This section explains the use of *sattva* to refer to tiny creatures, citing a passage from Vi 769a10-16.

**[3175] "chidrācchidreṣu"**

* This phrase is analyzed as an Āmreḍita compound, similar to *phalāphala*.

**[3176] Translations and Interpretations of the Verse**

* Various translations are critiqued for their inaccuracies or misunderstandings.
* The author favors Yasui's translation but notes its slight deviation from the original text.
* The importance of protecting small creatures living in the bed is emphasized, based on Tibetan and Chinese versions.





##  施護 *Dānapāla, tätig 982–1017)*

* **Fleischverzehr und sexuelle Begierde**
    * Nahrungseraufnahme erzeugt Vitalität, die zu Triebdruck führt.
    * Fleischverzehr verstärkt diese Vitalität und begünstigt sexuelle Begierde.
    * Alkoholkonsum und Knoblauch haben ähnliche Effekte.
    * Die Abhängigkeit des sexuellen Begehrens von Wunschvorstellungen wird erwähnt.

* **Die Folgen von Fleischkonsum und sexueller Begierde**
    * Verwirrung, Unvernunft und unmoralische Handlungen.
    * Wiedergeburt in einem Zustand des Leidens (Wehegeschrei-Hölle).

* **Das Konzept der *saṅgati***
    * Die genaue Bedeutung ist schwer zu bestimmen.
    * Mögliche Interpretationen: 
        * Fortdauer im Kreislauf von Tod und Wiedergeburt (*saṁsāra*)
        * Sexueller Verkehr
        * Zusammentreffen verschiedener Faktoren, die zur Wiedergeburt führen

* **Die Rolle des Geldes**
    * Geld wird verwendet, um Fleisch zu kaufen.
    * Sowohl Käufer als auch Verkäufer tragen Verantwortung für das Leid, das durch den Fleischkonsum verursacht wird.




## Bo 564B13:

* **Verse 10–11:** Missing in Chinese translations.

* **Verse 12a-b:** Two groups of people fall into the Weeping Hell due to their evil deeds after death.

* **Verse 12c-d:** The text discusses the concept of "cooked/roasted" in relation to hell, suggesting a possible textual variant.

* **Verse 13a-b:** Some modern translations accurately reflect the original Sanskrit meaning, highlighting that those who commit evil deeds are condemned.

* **Verse 13c-d:** This verse criticizes people who justify eating meat by citing Vinaya rules and argue that Buddha allowed it if the meat is "triple pure."

* **Verse 14a-b:** The text explains that eating meat leads to both worldly and spiritual ruin, as it violates Buddhist teachings.

* **Verse 14c-d:** Jñānavajra interprets "both worlds" (lokadvaya) as referring to the producer and consumer of meat.

* **Verse 15a-b:** The verse suggests that those who disregard wise words and eat meat do so out of ignorance, leading them astray from Buddha's teachings.

* **Verse 15c-d:** The text defines "triple pure" meat as meat that has not been seen, heard, or doubted to have been killed for consumption. However, it emphasizes that such meat does not exist.

* **Verse 16a-b:** This verse criticizes those who eat meat, stating that they are condemned by Buddhas.

* **Verse 16c-d:** The text explains that eating meat leads to rebirth in animal realms, particularly as carnivorous creatures.

* **Verse 17a-b:** Those who consume their own kind are reborn into animal forms like tigers and wolves.

* **Verse 17c-d:** The verse describes the consequences of eating meat, including foul smell, aversion, and mental instability in this life and rebirth as a low caste being in the next.





## Summary

**2.** This section discusses the meaning of the term *kakṣ(y)a* in the title of the *Hastikakṣyasūtra*. It presents different interpretations from Tibetan and Chinese translations, highlighting variations and challenges in understanding the term.

**3.** This section focuses on the consequences of meat-eating as described in the LaṅkS 8. It explores the various negative rebirths associated with this action, including being born as a Caṇḍāla, hunter, or Ḍomba.

**4.** This section analyzes the textual variations and interpretations regarding the birthplaces of those who consume meat. It examines different readings and their implications for understanding the verse's meaning.

**5.** This section delves into the specific rebirth possibilities mentioned in the LaṅkS 8, including being born as a Ḍākinī, Rākṣasa, or other lower beings. It discusses textual variations and possible interpretations of these rebirths.

**6.** This section examines the term *asau narādhamaḥ* and its meaning within the context of the verse. It analyzes different readings and their implications for understanding the intended message.

**7.** This section explores the various animal forms mentioned as potential rebirths for those who consume meat. It discusses textual variations and the significance of these animals in Buddhist cosmology.

**8.** This section continues the discussion on the term *kakṣ(y)a* from the title of the *Hastikakṣyasūtra*. It presents further evidence from Tibetan and Chinese translations, highlighting the ongoing debate surrounding its meaning.





## Summary of Text

**[1.] Title and its Meaning:**

* The Sūtra's title, *Hastikakṣa-sūtra*, is discussed, with various interpretations proposed.
* It might refer to the "Elephant's Girth" or "Elephant's Strength/Power."
* The meaning of *kakṣya* (part of the title) is debated, with possibilities including "Anstrengung," "Leibgurt," or "Kraft."

**[2.] Textual Evidence:**

* Several textual sources are cited to support different interpretations of the title.
* These include Tibetan translations, Chinese versions, and Sanskrit fragments.
* A passage from the Sūtra itself is analyzed, where it mentions individuals achieving the strength of an elephant.

**[3.] Connection to Elephant Imagery:**

* The text emphasizes the imagery of elephants, particularly their strength and powerful gait.
* This imagery is linked to the spiritual progress and authority of those who follow the teachings of the Sūtra.

**[4.] Etymological Analysis:**

* The etymology of *kakṣya* is explored, with references to Sanskrit dictionaries and grammatical rules.
* Different meanings are considered, including "Gürtel," "Kraft," and "Anstrengung."

**[5.] Proposed Interpretation:**

* Based on textual evidence and etymological analysis, the author suggests that *Hastikakṣa-sūtra* could mean "Lehrrede, [die diejenigen, welche ihr folgen,] wie der Leibriemen eines Elefanten (Tatp.) 
[kraftvoll ausschreiten läßt]" or "Lehrrede, [dank derer man (/der Bodhisattva)] wie ein Elefant gegürtet (Bahuv.), [kraftvoll ausschreitet und seine Aufgabe energisch in Angriff nimmt]."

**[6.] Other Topics:**

* The text briefly mentions a prohibition against meat consumption within the *Hastikakṣa-sūtra*.
* It also refers to the *Nirvāṇa-sūtra* and its connection to the Sūtra.





# 3220

- The author may have allowed for an irregular Sandhi in the LaṅkS.
- An irregular formation ăṅgulimālaka- (loosely analogous to Pāṇ 5.2.62?) or (if the otherwise secondary manuscripts in this point preserve the older form) ăṅguli*mālika*- might not be impossible in this text.

# 3221

- The LaṅkS may have been edited after its initial composition, with additions and changes made over time.
- Gu's version suggests that the reference to the (*Mahāpari*)- nirvāṇa(-*mahāsūtra*) was added later.

# 3222

- Gu: "in this Laṅkāsūtra [here]".

# 3223

- *Vivarjitam*, normally "abandoned, avoided," here perhaps in the sense of "[from the allowed foods] excluded."
- Gu and Śi 制斷, probably = "[forbidden, prohibited];" cf. also Gut (P ngu 313a1) *rab tu bkag* "strictly forbidden" and Bo 不聽 "not permitted."

# 3224

- This addition is also found in Bo (see En. 3225).

# 3225

- Gu (514b9): "eaten, without subsequently feeling shame" (食 已無慚愧; similarly Śi 624c1; cf. also Bo 564c23: 食肉無慚愧 "eats meat without feeling shame"), which is more easily compatible with the reading *nirlajja* of Śikṣ 133,7 than with LaṅkS *nairlajjyād*.
- Tib (P ngu 172b1: ngo mi tsha bar za ba [F1 na] ni) also gives the expression modally.

# 3226

- Gu (514b9): "... he is regularly (/always again) foolish and senseless" (生生常癡冥); Bo (564b23) and Śi (624c1) similarly, but "crazy" (顛狂, 癲狂) instead of "foolish and senseless."
- Cf. also verse 14b.

# 3227

- This verse stands at the very end of the chapter in the Chinese versions (an afterthought?).

# 3228

- Most manuscripts directly or indirectly support the Optative (*jāyeta*), which may be induced by the meter and here perhaps merely explicitly states that the realization of the verbal concept is conditional (namely, on abstaining from meat consumption) and thus, absolutely speaking, merely a possibility and not an independently ascertainable fact.
- The Indicative *jāyate* in T1, Po, and N11 is metrically problematic (old Vedic Triṣṭubh).

# 3229

- Rebirth in a Brahman family as a goal may seem strange from a Buddhist perspective (cf. TOKIWA 1994: II 130 note 7), but it is understandable given the social prestige of Brahmans and the assumption that, at the time, Brahman circles were promoting abstinence from meat.
- The alternative rebirth (*athavā*) in a family of Yogins may, on the other hand, refer to families who were close to Buddhism, especially in the form represented by the LaṅkS.

# 3230

- Gu (514b24f) has slight shifts: "That one is reborn in a Brahman family and among those who cultivate the [path of salvation] (= Yogins), (1...[and in one with] wisdom, wealth, and reputation [endowed],...1), that comes from not eating meat."

# 3231

- *Dṛṣṭa* and *śruta* could be understood here as Nomina actionis (hint W. Knobl).

# 3232

- The three terms are the counterpart of the traditional three conditions (*trikoṭi*), which must be met for ordained persons to be allowed to accept meat (see St. Chap. I.1.3.1).
- The composite is therefore certainly to be understood with Tib (P ngu 172b2: *mthong dang thos dang dogs pa yis*) as a three-membered Dvandva, and not as "with doubts about what is seen and heard" (CLEARY, HUANG).

# 3233

- TOKIWA adds: "that meat is offered as food" (1994: I 273,16: 肉が食事に供されるのを) or "that meat has been cooked" (2003b: 459,17: "meat cooked"), i.e., presumably that the offered meal contains meat.
- Then one would rather expect in Pāda b: "then he should refuse the offered meal."

# 3234

- Cf. DUNDAS 1992: 152: "It is of course open to question whether there can be a totally pure act of giving in which donor and recipient are free from any sense of expectation and there is no possibility of any act of violence" (emphasis L.S.).

# 3235

- WALDSCHMIDT (1967: 106) translates: "All meat should be avoided, the dialecticians from families of meat eaters do not understand this with (their limitation to) 'seen,' 'heard,' and 'suspicion.'"
- WALDSCHMIDT's proposal to construct Pāda a with c seems rather daring to me and is also not confirmed by Tib or the Chinese versions.

# 3236

- As a response to the reasoning of the *tārkika*s in verse 19?

# 3237

- The Potentialis *bhavet* here and in the next line could be explained, if one does not simply attribute its use to metrical constraints, as an expression of implied conditionality: If one gives room to desire or indulges in meat and alcohol consumption, then that hinders liberation.
- However, one can also resist temptation.

# 3238

- In all LaṅkS manuscripts, the subject is in the Pl. (*māṁsamadyādyā(ḥ*), mask. instead of neutr. [cf. BHSG # 8.100] m.c.?), but the predicate is m.c. in the Sing.

# 3239

- Gu (514b13) and Bo (564b27) insert "leeks, chives, and garlic" (葱韮蒜) instead of "etc."

# 3240

- Gu and Bo: "the Noble Path" (or "Path of the Noble," see DP I 236a)" (聖道, i.e., the path of salvation, *āryamārga*); similarly Śi (624c8): "If someone eats it, he cannot enter the *āryamārga*" (若有食之者 不能 入聖道).

# 3241

- Cf. B.8 (*mokṣavighnakaratv*āt) and E.24c as well as, implicitly, A.2, D.3.2, and E.7-8.
- Whether the verse specifically alludes to the idea that the latter strengthens sexual desire (see En. 3181) with the parallelization of desire and "meat, alcohol, etc." is left open.

# 3242

- Gu (514b14):"beings" (眾生), here = "persons."
- Bo (564b28) and Śi also.

# 3243

- Gu (= Bo u. Śi): "those who hold a ... view with regard to meat[consumption]" (於肉愚癡說); based on a reading *m*āṁsādau* ("with regard to meat, etc.") instead of *māṁsādā(ḥ*)??
- But Gut (P ngu 313a3) *sha za ... rnams* ≈ Tib (P ngu 172b3) sha za ... dag = *māṁsādāḥ*.

# 3244

* Some scholars argue that the Buddha taught meat is blameless and permissible.
* Others interpret *anuvarṇita* as coordinated with *kalpikaṁ nirāvadyaṁ*, suggesting meat-eating is acceptable, blameless, and approved by the Buddha.
* Gu seems to understand *buddhānuvarṇita* in this way.

# 3245

* Bo and Śi explicitly state that pure (i.e., permissible) food includes even meat.

# 3246

* The comparative particle is missing in modern translations and Nanjio's text, where it is replaced by *māṁsam*.
* Only Yasui adds the particle, but his reading of T1 (*bhaiṣajyaṁ māṁsam āhāram*) lacks support from other sources.

# 3247

* See **B.13**.1 with En. 2988 and 2989.

# 3248

* See **B.12**.3 with En. 2981.

# 3249

* See **B.12**.4 with En. 2987.
* Huang's representation of *pratikūlaṁ* as 不食肉 ("does not eat meat") is semantically problematic and ignores the historical context.

# 3250

* The syntax of the verse presents difficulties.
* One interpretation suggests that *piṇḍaṁ (samā)caret* should be understood as "going for alms" or "seeking alms food," with *mātrayā* modal and *pratikūlam* predicative.

# 3251

* See B.8 and D.3.
* Gu's version of the first line of the verse is: "To those who abide in the attitude of *maitrī*, I preach constant aversion (literally, disgust-and-aversion) [from meat-eating]."
* It is unclear if Gu's version is based on a different Sanskrit text.

# 3252

* This interpretation of the line is supported by Gu and Bo, who state that it is possible to constantly live with tigers, wolves, and other normally ferocious animals.

* **Band 10:** Mario Poceski (ed.), *Communities of Memory and Interpretation Reimagining and Reinventing the Past in East Asian Buddhism*

## Fleischverzehr und Vegetarismus im indischen Buddhismus bis ca. zur Mitte des ersten Jahrtausends n. Chr.

This work addresses the traditional stance of mainstream early Indian Buddhism on the topic of meat consumption. Meat and fish are generally permitted for both ordained individuals and laypeople, and there is little reason to believe that this was different in the earliest Buddhism. However, issues arise within the context of ethics (non-harming), asceticism, and social conventions, leading to restrictions and dissent. Despite some starting points in the older tradition, a distinctly (lacto-)vegetarian movement documented by its own texts becomes discernible only around 400 CE within Indian Mahāyāna Buddhism. The presentation and interpretation of the ideas, arguments, and motives of this movement are at the center of this work, and the crucial sections of the relevant texts are included in the form of extensive annotated translations and critical editions.

The work consists of three parts. The first part comprises an in-depth study of the topic of meat consumption and vegetarianism in Indian Buddhism until around 500 CE, as well as translations of the most important vegetarian texts. The second part contains, in the form of footnotes, supporting material, justifications, and explanations for this study and the translations. The third part includes critical editions of the texts translated in Part 1, as well as an abbreviations and bibliography.

**Part 2: Footnotes**
